FOREST RESOURCES MANAGEMENT PROJECT
Credit and Proiect Summary
Borrower:             People's Republic of Bangladesh
Amount.:               SDR 36.4 million (US$49.6 million equivalent)
Terms:                Standard, with a maturity of 40 years
Project Description:  Tre project's primary objectives would be to: (a) estab-
lish and maintain a forest management system that Is
fully respor-ive to the economic, environmental and
social goals of the country; (b) improve the productivi-
ty of Government-owned forests in orde -o meet, as much
as possible, the country's wood and e?      needs; and
(c) protect the country's environment.  _. s would be
achieved by: (i) improving and further institutionaliz-
ing the forest resource information management system
evolved under the Second Forestry Project (Cr. 1634-BD);
(ii) integrating environment and socio-economic factors
and criteria into resource management and utilization;
(iii) carrying out a pilot scheme for people's partici-
pation in forest development; (iv) expanding the coun-
try's forest resources through plantation establishment
(59,780 ha) and improved management; (v) formulating
management plans for nature conservation areas and
supporting investments in selected sanctuaries and
parks; (vi) streamlining the organization of the Forest
Department, and creating within the Department a new
responsibility for environmental management; and
(vii) providing support for professional and technical
education and training, and for mangrove research. The
project would finance civil works, vehicles, boats,
equipment, furniture, materials, service contracts,
technical assistance, training, labor, and incremental
staff and operating costs.
Benefits and Risks:   A major direct benefit from the project would be the
production of about 15 million m3 of wood which would
help maintain the country's supply of wood for indus-
trial use and fuel consumption. The project would also
make significant institutional improvements to ensure
that forest development is sustainable and environmen-
tally, socially and scientifically sound. Significant
environmental and social benefits would be expected Erom
the project's nature conservation and participatory
forest development activities. The project would pro-
vide 1,150 permanent staff positions and create labor
requirements for 83,500 person years. There are no
major risks. However, natural events, especially cy-



-iv-
clones and storm surges, could damage young and estab-
lished plantations. Due to wlde spacial distribution of
coastal plantations, this damage is likely to be limit-
ed. Also, in case of such natural disasters, the plan-
tations would normally provide protection to inland
propertles which might otherwise experience greater
losses. Plantations could also be damaged by uncon-
trolled activities of communities living in the vicinity
of the plantations. This risk would be minimized by
promoting and establishing people's participation in
resource management and utilization. Finally, poor
performance of implementing agencies could reduce the
level of the expected benefits. Technical assistance,
training and monitoring provided under the project to
improve institutional performances would address this
risk.

BANGLIDESH
FOREST RESOURCES MANAGEMENT PROJECT
I. SECTOR ALND PROJECT BACKGROUND
A.  frZnstrt  in the Economy
1.1       In 1990, the fore>-    sector contributed about Tk 28 billion to the
country's economy, or 3.51 of  DP. This statistic does not, however, reflect
the true importance of forestry in Bangladesh. To measure the sector's full
contribution, one needs to add: (a) the subsidies provided for state-owned
industries through controlled prices of wood raw material (para. 4.10);
(b) the 'arge quantities of fuelwood, fodder, thatching grass and other minor
forest products taken by villagers; and (c) illicit fellings. Forests also
play an important role in protecting watershed and irrigation structures, in
reclaiming land from the sea, in protecting coastal areas, and in the overall
environment. The sector employs about 2X of Bangladesh's labor force.
B. Forest Cover and Types
1.2       Some three million ha, or about 24X of Bangladesh's total land
area, is classified as forest. The Government-owned forest area is about two
million ha; the rest consists of privately controlled village forests and
homestead plantations spread throughout the country. The latter are of major
importance in supplying most of the bamboo (90X), timber (701) and fuelwood
(801) used by the rural population. The Government-owned forest area
comprises almost 1.5 million ha of natural forests and plantations under the
control of the Forest Department (FD) in the Ministry of Environment and
Forests (MOEF), and some 0.6 million ha of unclassified state forest
administered by the Ministry of Land through local authorities.
1.3       Forest land controlled by FD comprises 660,000 ha of hill forests,
located in Chittagong, Cox's Bazar, Sylhet and the Chittagong Hill Tracts
region; 120,000 ha of sal (Shorea robusta) forest, located in the center of
the country; and 570,000 ha of mangrove forests, located in the deltaic zone
of Sundarbans and in small areas of the Chittagong region's coastal belt. In
addition, since 1965, FD has established about 104,000 ha of mangrove
plantations in coastal areas along the Bay of Bengal and on offshore islands.
1.4       The hill forests have suffered from shifting cultivation and
illegal fellings. Various estimates indicate that about half of the forest
area under the control of FD has no tree cover. Most of these forests are
located in the Chittagong Hill Tracts, where Government control is limited due
to chronic civil disturbances. The sal forests, located in FD's Dhaka and
Mymensingh forest divisions, were significantly damaged, illicitly felled and
encroached due to population pressure and disturbances after the Liberation
War of 1971. Sundarbans is a large contiguous and isolated mangrove forest
block which is well preserved despite 100 years of commercial utilization.
1.5       The timber species of the natural forests are mainly Garjan
(DiRterocarpus M=) and Civit (Swintonia floribunda). There are also several
species of bamboo, which are of commercial importance. Sundri (Heritiera
minor), Gewa (Excoecaria agallocha), Baen (Avicennia officinalis) and Keora
(Sonneratia aRetala) are the main timber species of the mangrove forests.



- 2 -
Teak (Toctona zramnis) has been widely used in establishing early plantations.
There are also plantations of indigenous species such as Garjan and Dakijam.
More recently, EuclalDtus and Acscia species have been used for plantation
establishment. According to FD records, the total plantation area, including
mangrove plantations, is about 178,000 ha. However, the forest inventory
taken in 1985 classified about 9,000 ha of this area as failed plantations.
C. Production and Consumotion
1.6       Annual wood p-3duction is estimated at about 9.5 million m3, of
which 80X is used for fuel and 20% is converted primarily to sawnwood. About
801 of the production come from private sources, mainly homestead woodlots.
In recent years the annual production from Government-owned forests averaged
440,000 m3 of timber, 800,000 m3 of fuelwood and 90 million pieces of bamboo,
representing only about 20X of total wood production, and revealing the low
productivity of the state forests. Annual per capita wood consumption in
Bangladesh is about 0.1 m3, one of the lowest in the world. With Bangladesh's
population projected to increase to 145 million by the year 2000, per capita
consumption is likely to decline further. To help meet the country's wood
requirements, it is crucial for the Government to increase the productivity of
FD-managed forests and to expand FD's forest plantations.
1.7       Energy consumption in rural areas is dominated by biomass fuels
including firewood, agricultural residues and dung, which are readily
available and cheap. The consumption of other fuels constitutes only a small
fraction of total consumption in rural areas and is mainly limited to natural
gas. Because of the skewed distribution of the forests, wood is not available
in most rural areas; thus, agricultural residues and dung provide about 901 of
rural energy sources. Ir. contrast, agro-processing industries and commercial
entities (such as bakeries and brick kilns) mostly use fuelwood. 1/
1.8       Due to Bangladesh's wood shortage and the present ban on logging in
natural forests (para. 1.23), domestic market prices for wood are higher than
world prices. However, prices charged by FD to state-owned sawmills and pulp
and paper industries are generally well below world prices (para. 4.10).
D. Nature Conservation
1.9       There are 15 protected areas in Bangladesh.  With the exception of
the Sundarbans wildlife sanctuaries (para. 2.27), these areas have been
significantly degraded from many sources. In particular, illegal logging,
watercourse diversion, land clearing and poaching of wildlife have
significantly reduced the value of biological resources at some sites. Most
areas are poorly protected due to inadequate staff and facilities; lack of
management plans; and insufficient institutional capacity, especially within
FD (para. 2.32). Also, there is a dearth of current and correct information
on the environmental value and biodiversity of the protected areas.
1/ The use of fuelwood in brick manufacturing is banned, but the ban is
largely ineffective.



-3
1.10      Three wildlife sanctuaries in Sundarbans, totaling 32,386 ha, were
established in 1977 under the Bangladesh Wildlife (Preservation) Act of 1973.
The sanctuaries are managed under a plan, which waS prepared in 1983 uander
joint sponsorship of the World Wide Fund for Nature and the National
Zoological Park of the SLlthsonian Institution. This plan emphasizes the
preservation of tigers and other wildlife as an integral part of forest
management. Implementation of the plan, however, is suffering from several
constraints, including insufficient staff and infrastructure facilities
(para. 2.27).
E. Forestry Institutions
Forest and Environmental Management
1.11      Ministry of Environment and forests.  MOEF has overall
responsibility for the development of the forestry sector. It has
jurisdiction over FD, the Department of Environment (DOE), the Bangladesh
Forest Research Institute (BFRI), and the Bangladesh Forest Induscries
Development Corporation (BFIDC). The Ministry was established in 1989 through
the merger of the Department of Environmental Pollution Control, which had
been in the Ministry of Local Government and Rural Development, and the
Department of Forestry, formerly part of the Ministry of Agriculture. MOEF is
in the process of becoming fully established, but is still weak, particularly
in the following areas: (a) monitoring and evaluating the operations of its
executing agencies; (b) inter-agency coordination; and (c) resolution with
other ministries of important inter-sectoral issues, including land
allocation, wood prices, and human resource development.
1.12      Forest DeRartment.  FD is the primary Government agency responsible
for day-to-day management of forest resources in Bangladesh. The Department
is headed by a Chief Conservator of Forests (CCF). At FD's headquarters, the
CCF is assisted by three Deputy Chief ronservators (DCCF), responsible for
development planning, forest extension, and forest management planning,
respectively. Each DCCF is supported by an Assistant Chief Conservator of
Forests (ACCF). Also reporting to the CCF is a Conservator of Forests (CF)
who, with the assistance of two ACCFs, is responsible for general
administration and wildlife. Furthermore, the Directors of FD's Forest
Development and Training Center and the Upazila Afforestation and Nursery
Development Project (para. 1.30) are directly responsible to the CCF.
1.13      FD's field operations, consisting of six Circles headed by CFs and
concerned with territorial forestry, also come under tho CCF's direction. The
CF of each Circle is in charge of several forest divisions, which are normally
within the boundary of an administrative district, and under the charge of a
Divisional Forest Officer (DFO). There are 37 forest divisions, including one
covering the National Botanical Garden. The divisions are divided into
several forest ranges which are controlled by Forest Range Officers, who are
in charge of several beats, each under a Deputy Ranger or Forester. The field
staff is responsible for the establishment and maintenance of plantations,
supervision of state forest lands, provision of industrial wood supplies, and
extension and public relations at the local level.
1.14      At end-1991, FD's staff was about 10,000, including 4,850 technical
and professional staff. In addition, FD employs a labor force of about



- 4 -
500,000. The existing 'rganization of the Department (Chart I) is based -
traditional administration-oriented management with accountability focusing
mainly on adherence to budget allocations. Because work quality and
effectiveness are not systematically measured, the performance of FD's field
organization varies widely. The structure also relies exte.asively on the CCF
for control of and decision-making on all forestry aspects, thereby reducing
his effectiveness in auch vital areas as planning and programming. Moreover,
the organization is not sufficiently developed along functional lines,
combining, for instance, administration with wildlife activities and lacking
adequate integra.ior. of overlapping activities. In general, FD's headquarters
is weak in its planning, programming, monitoring, evaluating and environmental
management capabilities. Also, the Department's effectiveness is severely
hampered by a shortage of technical staff at the middle and upper management
levels. Furthermore, FD has been slow to introduce people-oriented forestry
development, which, in view of the country's extreme population p -sure, is
crlcial for the establishment, protection, and utilization of f    .s.  While
a ch nge in attitude toward people's participation is already eN--ant, clear
policies, institutional changes and proper programs have yet to be developed.
1.15      DepaXtment of Enironmgnt.  DOE is Bangladesh's only agency
specifically directed to exercise environmental regulatory authority. Its
mandate has recently been substantially broadened to include a strong role in
formulating environmental guidelines, preparing environmental assessments, and
advising line agencies whose activities affect soil and water conservation,
afforestation, wildlife, fisheries and other natural resource uses.
eorest Industries
1.16      The primary processing industries for forest products include
1,634 sawmills, 4 pulp and paper mills, 13 board mills, 2 timber treatment
plants, and 22 match faictories. Of these, the pulp and paper mills, one
hardboard mill, one particle board mill and four match factories are owned and
operated by the Bangladesh Chemical industries Corporation (BCIC), a
parastatel company. BFIDC, which is under MOEF;s control (para. 1.11),
manages one large sawmill, the timber treatment plants, one particle board
plant, one hardboard mill, one plywood mill, and the heavy logging industry in
the Chittagong Hill Tracts. The remaining 8 board mills, 18 match factories
and 1,632 sawmills are privately owned. The primary forest industries sector
is characterized by a shortage of raw material in relation to the installed
manufacturing capacity. In additio.i, the major large-scale industries face
problems resulting from inferior equipment and lack of trained personnel. The
private small-sized sawmills, however, have demonstrated good ability to adapt
to the fluctuating supply and demand situation.
F. Forestry Education and Training
1.17      General.  FD is responsible for training all technical forestry
staff such as rangers, foresters and forest guards. The Institute of Forestry
of the Chittagong University (IFCU) provides professional forestry education.
At present, the two agencies are unable to meet the sector's education and
training needs due to insufficient experienced teaching staff, lack of staff
continuity and career incentives, and severe shortages of facilities,
equipment and books. As a result, many FD staff members, particularly
foresters and forest guards, have not had formal training in forestry.



-5-
1..L8     University Education. IFCU offers a four-year university-level
education in forestry leadtng to a B.Sc. (Hons) degree.  The Institute admits
about 35 students arnually, and some 90 students have been graduated over the
past three years. During the Fourth Five-Year Plan period (FY91-FY95), about
150-175 students are expected to graduate from IFCU. The Institute is flow
planning to introduce two additional career paths: one leading to an M.Sc.
degree in forestry; the other to a B.Sc. degree in environmental science.
1.19      Forest College Training.  Until 1985, the Chittagong Forest College
of FD provided forest rangers a two-year course leading to a B.Sc. (Pass)
degree in forestry. The minimum entry qualification was a B.Sc. (Pass) degree
in natural sciences. Because of FD's 1985 decision to abolish the sub-
professional ranger cadre, the College is discontinuing the ranger course and
will be converted into FD's in-service training institute. As part of its new
role, the College will offer in-service training for professional forest
graduates and refresher courses for FD's staff. For a limited period, it will
also continue upgrading existing forest rangers to professional foresters.
1.20      Forest School Training.  The forest schools at Sylhet (established
in 1948) and at Rajshahi (established in 1985), both under the control of FD,
offer two-year diploma courses for foresters and three-month courses for
forest guards. The schools have combined accommodation for about 100
foresters and 30 forest guards. FD recently decided to change the entry level
requirement for forester candidates to 10 years of high school (from the
current 12 years) and increase course duration to 3 years. For forest guards,
training courses will be increased to 4 months' duration.
G. Forest Research
1.21      Bangladesh Forest Research Institute.  BFRI was established at
Chittagong in 1968 by enlarging the then-existing Forest Research Laboratory.
BFRI has two research branches (the Forest Products Branch with 6 research
divisions, and the Forest Management Branch with 13 research divisions) and a
General Services branch with four divisions. All three branches report
directly to the Director. BFRI's staff strength is almost 700, divided among
its 2 research branches, the General Services branch, and the Institute's
17 field research stations. The Institute is working in isolation with
virtually no coordination with FD or other user agencies. As a result, BFRI's
potential has not been fully utilized. Research priorities are too diverse,
and research efforts are neither field-oriented nor geared to users' needs.
H. Government Plans and Policies
1.22      The Government of Bangladesh's (GOB's) overall objectives in the
forestry sector, as outlined in its Fourth Five-Year Plan (FY91-FY95), are to
optimize sustainable production of goods and services from the existing
limited forest lands, expand the current forest cover and improve the overall
environmental condition of the country. GOB's strategy aims to improve the
forests' productive and protective base through better management, investment
in coastal plantations and rehabilitation of degraded forest areas, and
through promoting people's participation in the management and utilization of
the forest resources. Proposed actions focus on: (a) bringing all forest
resources under objective-oriented management; (b) establishing plantations,



- 6 -
including coastal plantations, to stabilize newly accreted lands and protect
from cyclones; (c) identifying and managing protective areas to conserve
nature; (d) imuproving planning, implementation, monitoring and environmental
management capabilities of sector institutions; and (e) expanding the
information base by undertaking appropriate research programs. GOB also
promotes farm/community forestry. The Government's objectives and strategy
are in line with IDA's forest sector pol.icy and are supported by the donor
community. 2/
1.23      To help achieve its se.-tor objectives, GOB has included in its
Fourth Five-Year Plan a series of planned investments in forest plantations,
forestry education and training, fo-rest research, and nature conservation.
The proposed Forest Resources Management Project is part of GOB's overall
program, whose cost is estimated at Tk 8.5 billion (US$220 million
equivalent). GOB's ability to implement the program will largely depend on
the availability of donor funding. GOB is also preparing a Forestry Sector
Master Plan (para. 1.32) to develop a long-term perspective for the sector.
In support of its conservation efforts, GOB has ordered a ban on logging in
natural forests, which has had only limited success -- indeed it has led to a
significant increase in illegal logging. GOB is therefore considering lifting
the ban.
I. IDA's Sector Assistance Strategy
1.24      IDA's sector dialogue with GOB and its financial support to the
sector have concentrated on the need to ensure a sustainable supply of forest
products and services. In this context, the emphasis of IDA's previous
assistance has been on developing forest plantations, better managing existing
forest resources, and improving the effectiveness of sector institutions.
While these aspects continue to be the thrust of IDA's sector assistance
strategy, experience from the implementation of IDA-financed forestry projects
in Bangladesh (paras. 1.25-1.29) and from elsewhere has made it clear that a
broader approach is required for successful forest development. In its sector
strategy, IDA is therefore also emphasizing the need for reforms in the
following areas: (a) environment and nature conservation, (b) forest land use
and people's participation in sector development, and (c) pricing of forest
products sold to public sector agencies.
1.25      Mangrove Afforestation Project.  IDA started its financial support
of forest develupment in Bangladesh with the Mangrove Afforestation Project
(Cr. 1042-BD; Credit amount: US$11 million), which was approved in June 1980
and implemented over five-and-a-half years. The main project objectives were
to stabilize accreted lands along the Bay of Bengal through the establishment
of 40,000 ha of mangrove plantations, and to increase the production of
fuelwood and industrial wood through management of the project's plantations
ar,d of 32,000 ha of previously established plantations. The project also
provided for (a) the establishment of a Plantation Trial Unit for applied
research; (b) the initiation of a program to monitor plantation establishment,
land accretion and erosion througn aerial photography and remote sensing; and
2   Under its forest sector policy, IDA finances forestry operations, if there
is government commitment to sustainable and conservation-oriented forestry.
Annex 1 summarizes the project's link to IDA's policy.



-7-
(c) the strengthening of forest training institutions. The project
established about 30,500 ha of new plantations and demonstrated the value of
remote sensing. It also helped to meet the increased forestry training needs.
The project's Plantation Trial Unit failed, however, mainly due to lack of
experienced staff, and the achievements in plantation management also fell
short of appraisal expectations. Overall, project implementation was
successful, with an 18% economic rate of return.
1.26      Second Forestry Project.  Upon completion of the Mangrove
Afforestation Project in 1985, IDA continued its support to the sector by
financing the ongoing Second Forestry Project (Cr. 1634-BD). A Credit of
US$28 million was approved in November 1985; implementation started in 1986;
and the Credit is scheduled to close in June 1992, one year later than
originally expected. The project aims at increasing the supply of forest
products by improving the productivity of natural forests and expanding FD's
plantation program. It provides for (a) establishment of 40,000 ha of
mangrove plantations, and maintenance of about 81,000 ha of existing mangrove
plantations; (b) establishment of 23,000 ha of industrial plantations, and
maintenance of 28,030 ha of existing industrial plantations; (c) development
of a resource management system; and (d) improvement of mangrove afforestation
research, and forestry training and education.
1.27      Overall project performance is satisfactory.  By September 1991,
mangrove planting and industrial forest plantations reached 35,200 ha and
19,800 ha, respectively (88% and 86%, respectively, of project targets), and
the remaining plantations are expected to be completed by mid-1992.
Maintenance of existing mangrove plantations is also proceeding well.
However, progress in thinning industrial plantations is slow, and only 60% of
the target has been achieved due to (a) priority given by FD to plantation
establishment, (b) inadequate local funds allocated by GOB, and (c) delays in
preparing forest management plans (FMPs). Under the project, FD has developed
a computerized Resource Information Management System (RIMS), which is capable
of continuously updating resource information and forecasting future resource
changes uncder various scenarios. Its data base has gaps, however, and further
research is needed on yields of tree species and stands to improve the basis
for forest management decisions. Implementation of the system has been slow
due to insufficient qualified personnel and inadequate training of field
staff.
1.28      The mangrove research program, carried out by BFRI, is progressing,
but it has had little impact so far, due to weak management and inadequate
coordination between BFRI and FD. The project's education program,
implem9nted by IFCU, is doing well, but FD has not started in-service training
at its Chittagong Forest College and has now stopped foresters' training
courses at its Sylhet Forest School due to indecision on the type of training
to be adopted and the limitation of physical facilities. The civil works
program at IFCU is in progress and is expected to be completed end-June 1992.
However, the civil works programs at Chittagong Forest College and Sylhet
Forest School were delayed because of GOB's indecision on FD's recruitment and
education policies (para. 3.6), and they can no longer be undertaken in the
time remaining until the Credit closes.
1.29      The experience gained under both projects underlines the importance
of a reliable data base and information system for effective development of
the forestry sector. It also points to the need for improved arrangements and



procedures in such areas as program development and approval, monitoring and
managezient accountability, and planning and budget preparation. During
project implementation, it has further become clear that forestry development
will not be sustainable unless local people and their needs are fully
integrated. In addition, environmental aspects must play a more prominent
role in the planning and decision-making process. The experience under these
projects has also underlined the need for sound policies on (a) forestry
education and recruitment, to enable FD to meet its professional and technical
staff needs and permit the establishment of a supportive education and
training infrastructure, and (b) pricing, to ensure proper valuation of forest
products sold to state-owned industries and thereby discourage inefficiency.
J. Sector Assistance by Other Donors
1.30      The Asian Development Bank (ADB) has provided significant
assistance to Bangladesh's forestry sector, concentrating mainly on social
forestry. ADB first financed a Community Forestry Project, which was
completed in 1987; it then supported the ongoing Upazila Afforestation and
Nursery Development Project. The latter, approved in 1989 and cofinanced by
the United Nations Development Programme (UNDP), covers 61 districts and
concentrates on tree planting on public non-forest land, on homesteads, along
roadsides, railways and canals, and on river and coastal embankments. The
project is being implemented primarily through local governments (Upazilas).
1.31      UNDP and the Food and Agriculture Organization of the United
Nations (FAO) have provided valuable technical assistance to the forestry
sector for over a decade. Some seven different projects have been
implemented. Technical assistance provided by UNDP and FAO under the Second
Forestry Project has been effective in (a) developing RIMS, and (b) preparing
several forest management plans which were approved by MOEF in late-1991.
UNDP is also financing the FAO-executed Integrated Resource Development of
Sundarbans Reserve Forest Project, which supports mangrove research and the
preparation of a new multiple-use manage.ment plan for Sundarbans.
Implementation of this project recently started and will be closely integrated
into the proposed Forest Resources Management Project.
1.32      ADB, UNDP and FAO are also providing funds to GOB to help formulate
a Forestry Sector Master Plan. Preparation of the Plan started in late-1991
and is scheduled to be completed in 1993. The Plan is expected to establish a
framework for the protection and development of forest resources that will
optimize the sector's long-term contribution to environmental stability and
economic and social development. In particular, it will (a) identify sectoral
issues and constraints; (b) prepare a long-term sector strategy and
development program; (c) design an institutional reform program for all
forestry institutions; and (d) define required policy reforms in areas such as
legislation, regulation, law enforcement, land use and allocation, and pricing
of forest products. The Plan will draw extensively on the policy dialogue
already carried out between GOB and IDA during the preparation of the propostd
Forest Resources Management Project; it is also expected to include the policy
and institutional reforms which would be covered under the project. IDA will
provide input at all critical stages of Plan preparation, and actively
participate in the resolution of policy and institutional issues which are not
addressed under the proposed project, such as rationalization of forest
industries.



- 9-
II.TH PRO~J.ECT
A. Rationale for IDA SunRort
2.1       The proposed project, which is a component of GOB's sector
investment program, would form an integral part of IDA's sectoral support
strategy, and would enable GOB and IDA to consolidate initiatives undertaken
in the first and second IDA-financed forestry projects (paras. 1.25-1.29).
IDA's involvement would help ensure that the improvements made and lessons
drawn from these projects would be fully integrated into the sector's
development and that experience from other countries could be brought to
Bangladesh's forestry operations. In particular, IDA would be able to
contribute to establishing (a) a functional and accountable FD, equipped with
up-to-date resource management technology and well-trained staff; (b) a sound
forest policy, which would be responsive to environmental and social goals and
realities of the country; (c) education and training facilities capable of
supplying the sector with qualified staff; and (d) an effectively managed and
protected area system for nature and environment conservation. Because of the
scope and extent of its past and current involvement in the sector, IDA has a
comparative advantage in assisting GOB to attain sound forest development.
2.2       The project would also be an instrument to achieve the development
and conservation goals outlined in the World Bank's forest sector policy,
especially with regard to (a) encouragement of sustainable resource use and
discouragement of deforestation, (b) forest resource expansion, (c) local
people's participation in forest management, and (d) nature preservation
(Annex 1). In addition, the project would assist IDA in achieving its poverty
alleviation objectives. The project would be implemented over a large area;
it would provide employment to rural people and improve their income by
adopting forest management systems which would be more responsive to local
needs. Continuation of IDA assistance to the forestry sector would also help
to more effectively channel assistance from other donor agencies into the
sector. The project, together with the ongoing ADB-assisted Upazila
Afforestation and Nursery Development Project (para. 1.30), would bring the
entire sector under a comprehensive and well-balanced development program.
B. Proiect Objectives and General Descrintion
Proiect Objectives
2.3       The project's primary objectives would be to (a) establish and
maintain a forest management system that is fully responsive to the economic,
environmental and social goals of the country; (b) improve the productivity of
Government-owned forests in order to meet, as much as possible, the country's
wood and energy needs; and (c) protect the country's environment. This would
be achieved by (i) improving and further institutionalizing RIMS, which
evolved under the Second Forestry Project; (ii) integrating environment and
socio-economic factors and criteria into resource ma~nagement; (iii) carrying
out a pilot scheme for people's participation in forest development;
(iv) expanding the country's forest resources through plantation establishment
and improved management; (v) formulating management plans for nature
conservation areas and supporting investments in selected sanctuaries and
parks; (vi) streamlining FD's organization, and creating within PD an



- 10 -
effective environmental management capability; and (vii) supporting
professional and technical education and training, and mangrove research.
General DescriRtion
2.4       The project, to be implemented over seven years i/, would include
the following components:
(a) Forest Resources Management. This component would help ensure that
the allocation and intensity of forest uses would be regulated in
accordance with appropriate forest management plans (FMPs). It
would establish additional planning capacity within FD; carry out
new forest resopurce inventories; improve data collection and
processing systems; prepare new FMPs, arl revise and update
existing FMPs, as required; develop and implement pilot activities
to involve local communities in forest development and protection;
and undertake silvicultural treatment of forest stands;
(b) Forest Resources Expansion. This component would fund some 60,000
ha of new mangrove and conventional forest plantations through
rehabilitating degraded forest areas, regenerating harvested
stands, and consolidating newly accreted land along the Bay of
Bengal. In addition, tending programs for young plantatio4is
established under the Second Forestry Project would be continued;
(c) Nature Conservation. Management plans would be prepared for all
national parks and wildlife sanctuaries where adequate natural
values exist. The component would meet the investment requirements
for the three Sundarbans sanctuaries and provide US$500,000 to help
meet investment needs in the remaining protected areas. The plans
for these areas would be used for soliciting additional funding
from international donor agencies; and
(d) Institutional Support. FD's headquarters would be reorganized and
its field organization expanded in line with the needs of FD's key
functions and the needs of the project. Also, a new Environmental
Management Wing would be set up within FD. The component would
further support education of professional foresters at IFCU, and
training of technical staff at FD's training facilities. BFRI
would receive assistance for continuing and expanding the mangrove
research started under the Second Forestry Project, and for
revitalizing its mangrove silviculture division in Khulna.
C.  Project Area
2.5       The project would have national coverage, except for plantation
activities in (a) the Chittagong Hill Tracts, where security conditions
prevent normal operations, and (b) the remnant sal forest north of Dhaka,
which is being supported under the ADB-financed Upazila Afforestation and
./ The implementation period has been determined on the basis of the
project's investment program and the experience gained under the first two
IDA-assisted forestry projects in Bangladesh (paras. 1.25-1.29).



- 11 -
Nursery Development Project (para. 1.30). The coastal afforestation divisions
of Bhola, Patuakhali, Noakhali and Chittagong would concentrate on the
establishment and management of the project's mangrove plantations, while the
forest divisions of Chittagong, Cox's Bazar, Sylhet and Khulna (Sundarbans)
would be concerned with the establishment and management of industrial
plantations and the management of natural forests. The project would not
cover investment and rationalization needs of the primary forest industries
sector. Key issues affecting this subsector will be addressed under the
Forestry Sector Master Plan (para. 1.32).
D. Detailed Features
Forest Resources Management (Base Cost: USS7.6 million)
2.6       Forest Management Plans (Base Cost: USS3.' million).  To ensure
sound forest management, FD would carry out future operations in accordance
with detailed FMPs. Progress has been made under the Second Forestry Project
in FMP preparation; and MOEF, in late-1991, approved the FMPs for the Sylhet,
Chittagong and Cox's Bazar forest divisions. FD has also prepared a manual
for the management of coastal plantations, and a Forest Management Manual,
which describes the procedures regulating the preparation, implementation and
revision of FMPs. An FMP for the Sundarbans has not yet been prepared.
2.7       While recent developments are encouraging, constraints are being
encounzered in the preparation, revision and implementation of FMPs. The two
existing management planning offices, in Chittagong and Dhaka, oversee an
excessively larga area and are inadequately staffed and equipped. As a
result, preparation of FMPs has been slow and is now based to large extent on
outdated inventory data. The project would strengthen FD's planning
capability through financing the establishment of a third management planning
office in Khulna, which would be responsible for the preparation, updating and
revision of FMPs for the Sundarbans mangrove forest and the four coastal
afforestation divisions. Funding would also be provided for additional
technical staff, facilities, vehicles and equipment required at FD's
headquarters and in the field to ensure the accuracy of forest inventories,
the effectiveness of photo interpretation, and the coordination between the
various management plan offices. The project would furthermore finance the
preparation of new forest inventories since existing ones are incomplete, up
tc nine years old, and no longer reliable due to unrecorded forest depletion,
fires, illegal fellings, diseases and encroachments. The inventory program
would cover the Chittagong, Cox's Bazar, Sylhet, and Sundarbans forest
divisions and the four coastal afforestation divisions. Forest inventories
would update forest c"ver and stand-related data and enable FD to design
appropriate and location-specific management systems responsive to the
country's economic, environmental and social objectives. The preparation of
new inventories would require strengthening of the mapping base to facilitate
digitizing of maps onto a new Geographical Information System (GIS)
(para. 2.9), and updating tree growth information. For Sundarbans and the
Chittagong, Cox's Bazar and coastal divisions, it would also involve new
aerial photography. Due to difficulties in flying near border areas, Sylhet
forest inventories would be prepared using ground-based methods.
2.8       The project would provide technical assistance to FD to help
(a) design'the inventory updating program and guide its execution (36 months);



- 12 -
(b) train FD staff in photo interpretation (8 months); (c) prepare new and
revise existing FMPs, and assist in their implementation (8 months);
(d) strengthen its cartographic mapping operations and prepare new maps
(45 months); and (e) upgrade existing computer capabilities to fully meet FD's
planning needs (26 months). The project would also finance materials and
incremental operating costs required to strengthen FD's forest management
planning operations.
2.9       Resource Information Management System (Base Cost: USSl.l million).
RIMS, which was established under the Second Forestry Project, would be
expanded and upgraded to a GIS-based system. Improvement of RIMS would aim at
(a) including socio-economic, environmental, and administrative information
into the data base (para. 2.32); and (b) modifying and expanding the
capebility of the system to respond to a larger spectrum of management goals
and  wider data base.  Building on the expanded data base and a broader set
of economic, social and environmental objectives, new FMPs would be prepared
and existing ones revised for about 700,000 ha of forest area within the Cox's
Bazar, Chittagong, Sylhet, Sundarbans and coastal divisions.
2.10      The project would finance the upgrading of existing equipment and
the addition of new equipment and staff required for GIS operations. It would
also provide necessary materials and transportation facilities, and finance
incremental operating costs. To assist in (a) acquiring and installing
suitable equipment and software, (b) designing and executing GIS training
programs for FD staff, and (c) monitoring the effectiveness of GIS use, FD
would periodically engage the services of a GIS specialist for a total of
about 14 months. In addition, the project would provide overseas and in-
service GIS training for selected FD staff.
2.11      Silvicultural Treatment (Base Cost: USSl.l million).  Maintenance
of the silvicultural health of existing plantations is essential for vigorous
tree growth and good plantation stocKing. Due to delay in preparing FMPs
(para. 1.27), many existing plantations have not been tended according to
their silvicultural treatment needs. As a result, some stands are deformed,
thinning yields have been foregone, and future harvesting volumes and wood
values will be reduced. In particular, climber cutting and thinning
operations have been inadequate. The project would finance the cost of
(a) climber cutting for about 37,600 ha of existing mangrove plantations, and
other silvicultural treatments for some 22,500 ha of these plantations, and
(b) thinning of about 29,000 ha of existing industrial plantations. Thinning
operations in industrial plantations would comprise first thinning (5,000 ha),
second thinning (17,900 ha), and third thinning (6,100 ha). Only the cost of
the first thinning would be fully financed under the project; for the second
and third thinnings, which are considered self-financing, the project would
fund only tree marking and supervision activities. The silvicultural
treatment program would be as follows:

2.12      To ensure effective planning and silvicultural treatment for
existing plantations, it would also be necessary to demarcate sub-block
boundaries which are presently lacking. In addition, access to and within the
existing plantations would need to be enhanced so that the plantations can be
better serviced. The project would finance the construction of 90 km of
roads, and the labor and materials required for survey and demarcation work.
2.13      ParticiRatorv Forest Develogment (Base Cost: USS1.7 million).  Over
tha past five years in Bangladesh, as in other countries in the region, there
has been an increasing awareness of the need to involve local populations in
forestry development and management activities, particularly in areas of dense
and growing population. Under initiatives supported by non-governmental
organizations (NGOs), limited experience has been gained with involving local
people in rthabilitation of degraded forest lands.  However, no initiatives
have been made in the project area. The project would, therefore, include a
pilot scheme for participatory forest development tc be implemented initially
in two forest divisions with hilly forest tracts in the project area. Based
on its experience, the component would be evaluated during the first
implementation review (para. 3.17), to determine a program (including
additional investment and financing needs) for expanding participatory
activities to other areas. To implement the component, FD would set up a
small pilot project unit under its Extension Wing, which is responsible for
social forestry programs. The unit would engage the services of a national or
international advisory NGO with expertise in participatory forestry. The NGO
would help FD to (a) formulate pilot strategies and options, (b) supervise
pilot activities by local-level NGOs who would be engaged for field
implementation, and (c) monitor and evaluate pilot operations. The advisory
NGO would also undertake a socio-economic survey in the pilot area and
organize training workshops. The unit would liaise with FD staff involved in
the Upazila Afforestation and Nursery Development Project (para. 1.30), which
is experimenting with participatory forestry outside the state forest, and
developing technical models for plantation/contract reforestation, forest
management, and agroforestry.
2.14      The pilot scheme would be undertaken in two or three forest blocks
in Chittagong and Cox's Bazar forest divisions. Areas would be identified
which represent the range of pressure on forest lands (different types of
settlers, different ethnic groups, different ecological conditions, different
range of forest-based economic activities being carried out) and groups of
settlers and villagers involved in forest management. Special measures would
be included in the scheme to ensure that participating women would function
not only as laborers, but also as active managers in pilot activities.
Optional models would include agroforestry, intensified taungya, contract
afforestation, and joint management of forest plots with local groups and FD.
A variety of legal and sharing arrangements would be tested. Upon completion
of the pilot phase in FY96, FD would develop model agreements and sharing
arrangements to be entered into with participatory groups on a larger scale.
2.15      During the design of the pilot scheme, information was collected on
tribal groups that reside in the project area or are dependent upon forest
products. While these groups have a strong tribal identity, they have been
relatively accultured to the dominant society. The socio-economic survey
(para. 2.13) would further expand the information base on the tribal
population and provide a basis for formulating management arrangements for
specific forest areas. If successful, the pilot activities would increase the



- 14 .
income of tribal households and strengthen those characteristics of their
society and culture which are based on traditional relationships to forest
resources. The results of the pilot scheme would also generate optional
models for participatory forest management in other areas of Bangladesh with
significant tribal populations. It was therefore considered unnecessary to
prepare a separate tribal development plan for the project.
2.16      The project would finance the establishment and operation of the
pilot project unit. Funds would be provided to finance the staff and
operating costs, computer and other equipment, and transport facilities
required for implementing this pilot operation. Also covered would be the
costs of services to be provided by the advisory NGO, and the implementation
assistance provided by local NG0s. To help FD review the NGO proposals and
monitor the effectiveness of their implementation, the project would provide
FD six months of technical assistance each in the areas of agroforestry and
agronomy, where FD lacks expertise. The World Food Program's (WFP)
Food-for-Work Program would reimburse the labor inputs by participating groups
(para. 2.44). Details on the pilot scheme are in Annex 2.
Forest Resources ExDansion (Base Cost: USS24.9 million)
2.17      General.  The project would support the establishment and
maintenance of 59,780 ha of new plantations comprising 32,900 ha of mangrove
and 26,880 ha of industrial plantations. Mangrove afforestation would be
carried out on newly accreted land in the Bay of Bengal (23,800 ha), and on
existing plantations which were severely damaged by the April 1991 cyclone
(9,100 ha). Industrial plantations would be established to revitalize denuded
and degraded forest lands and to regenerate harvested areas.g/ They would
include about 13,350 ha of long-rotation and 13,530 ha of short-rotation
species. For young plantations established under the Second Forestry Project,
the project would support maintenance operations required during the initial
years after planting. The program is summarized below:

2.18      Mangrove Afforestation (Base Cost: USS7.8 million).  During the
first two project years, emphasis would be given to rehabilitating cyclone
damaged plantation areas since these areas may become unsuitable for mangrove
afforestation if not planted within that time. Sites selected for plantation
establishment on newly accreted lands would be mapped beforehand and formal
allocation of these sites to FD would be obtained from the Ministry of Land
(MOL) (para. 3.11). All siter would be surveyed and the boundaries marked.
The present system for plantation establishment would be continued although
changes may be introduced as results from applied mangrove research become
available (para. 2.39). The principal species planted would be Keora
(Sonneratia apetala); Baen (Avicennia _fficinalis) would be limited to
locations with high salinity. The plantations would generally be managed
under a 10-year or a 17-year rotation cycle. However, because of the dynamic
character'of the coastal areas, actual rotation periods may be significantly
different.
2.19      The project would finance the cost of plantation establishment,
including nursery development and plantation maintenance for the first four
years after planting. It would also fund the additional professional and
techrical staff required by FD for implementing the program, and provide
necessary office and transport facilities, equipment, tools and materials.
Project financing would cover the construction of residential quarters for
staff working in remote areas where rental accommodation is unavailable.
2.20      Industrial Plantations (Base Cost: USS16.0 million).  Establishment
of industrial plantations requires advance planning in conformity with the
respective FMP. This would be achieved by surveying plantation areas at least
one year before work commences in order to prepare operational maps which
would detail plantation layout, sub-block boundary demarcation and road
construction. Plantations would be established under two management systems:
(a)  long rotation (about 40 years) primarily for the production of
sawlogs and poles; and
(b)  short rotation (12 and 18 years) for the production of poles,
peeler logs, fuelwood and pulpwood.
2.21      The long-rotation plantings would largely comprise Garjan
(Dipterocarpus UR), Dhakijam (Syzygium grande), Chapalis (ArtocarRus
chaplasha), Mahogany (Swietenia macorQhyDlaj), and Teak (Tectona grandis).
Teak would be confined to the better sites. Denuded, degraded sites would be
assigned to short-rotation species, in particular, the fast-growing Acacias:
A. mangium and A. auriculiforms, and Eucalyptus varieties. Short-rotation
species would produce round timber for transmission and general utility poles.
Some sawlogs would be produced from A. mangium. Experience under the Second
Forestry Project has shown that Eucalyptus is sensitive to site conditions and
requires intensive maintenance. Its share in the plantation program would
therefore be kept low. Melokana (Albizia falcataria) would be grown on good
alluvial soils in Sylhet for producing peeler logs and pulpwood on a 12-year
rotation.
2.22      Seedling production would be in accordance with conventional
nursery techniques, and planting would follow proven practices and established
tree spacing parameters. Young plantations would be weeded and fertilized,
and vacancies would be filled according to the requirements at individual



- 16 -
sites. In Europe and North America, improved seedling production techniques
have been developed using new seedling containers ("root-trainers"). To test
this technology under Bangladesh conditions and to develop improved rooting
material, a small experimental nursery, with capacity for 200,000
containerized seedlings, would be established in Chittagong forest division.
The nursery would also be used to train FD staff in nursery techniques.
2.23      Static fire protection would be provided involving the construction
and maintenance of firelines and breaks. The planned intensity of firebreaks
would be about 50 m per ha of plantation. The location of firebreaks would be
determined taking into account sub-block demarcation and road construction
needs. Guards would be employed in areas susceptible to fires.
2.24      Under the Second Forestry Project, FD developed a socio-economic
survey manual designed to achieve the maximum useful information from local
community contacts and to provide a means for analyzing people's attitudes
toward afforestation/reforestation and its objectives. However, the process
was used mainly for determining and avoiding sites which were potentially
problematic. Under the proposed project, a more positive approach to site
selection would be taken to increasingly facilitate people's participation in
the management, use and protection of forest resources. In addition, the
existing socio-economic survey manual would be expanded to make it more useful
in identifying poteatial resource expansion and management opportunities that
are dependent on the cooperation of local communities.
2.25      The project would finance the cost of industrial plantation
establishment and maintenance during the initial years after planting. It
would also fund the cost of vehicles, equipment, materials and incremental FD
staff required to carry out the program. In addition, it would provide office
facilities and housing for some of the incremental staff. The project would
furthermore finance the technical assistance services of (a) a rural
sociologist to help FD revise its socio-economic survey manual and its
approach to selecting plantation sites, and establish better relations with
local communities (9 months); (b) a soil conservationist to assist FD in
addressing soil erosion issues and to provide staff training in soil
conservation (6 months); and (c) a silviculturist to help FD develop improved
rooting material and nursery techniques (6 months).
2.26      Maintenance of Existing Plantations (Base Cost: USSl.l million).
Young plantations already established under the Second Forestry Project need
to be maintained under a system similar to that applied to plantations
established under the proposed project. Maintenance operations would be
required during the initial years after establishment and would include
activities such as filling vacancies, weeding and applying fertilizer. About
20.3 million seedlings would be required for infilling the existing young
mangrove plantations; for the existing industrial plantations, seedling
requirements would be about 2 million. The project would finance the labor
and material required for maintenance operations.
Nature Conservation (Base Cost: USS2.3 million)
2.27      The project would make a major contribution to a deeper and broader
integration of environmental considerations into the management of the state
forest. It would achieve this through (a) an institution-building activity,
to help establish in FD an effective environmental management capacity



- 17 -
(paras. 2.32-2.34); and (b) a nature conservation program, to support the
development and expansion of protected areas. Conservation activities would
focus mainly on intensified management and expansion of the existing wildlife
sanctuaries in Sundarbans, which have important natural values and which are
located where protection from encroachment and resource degradation is
feasible. The three sanctuaries, established in 1977, are Sundarbans West
(9,069 ha), Sundarbans South (17,878 ha) and Sundarbans East (5,439 ha).
Their importance for wildlife protection has increased over the last decades
as several species of reptiles have been heavily persecuted for their meat,
skins and eggs, and as the fish catches in Sundarbans have been dwindling.
The sanctuaries do not adequately protect the habitat types present in the
Sundarbans mangrove forest block, and they are also being encroached upon by
seasenal fishing camps. Their management suffers from (a) inadequate staffing
and poor infrastructure, (b) lack of trained personnel, (c) outdated
management plans, (d) unscientific management of the tiger and deer
populations, and (e) lack of fresh water supplies. To address these problems,
the project would expand the protected areas, improve the sanctuarie-'
infrastructure, and strengthen the management and protection capabilities of
FD's nature conservation staff. In particular, in Sundarbans East, the
sanctuary would be enlarged, the forest station at Katka would be upgraded to
sanctuary headquarters, and new stations would be established at the
sanctuary's northeast and northwest extremities. In the Sundarbans South
sanctuary, Hiron Point would be remodeled to a headquarters, and new stations
would be set up at the south end of Asraf Island and at the northern tip of
the sanctuary. Sundarbans West would be extended westward to the Indian
border to link up with India's Su-idarbans National Park and thus create the
potential for a cross-border reserve. The sanctuary, which has no permanent
facility, would be provided a headquarters and three stations. All
sanctuaries would be headed by Assistant Conservators of Forests (ACFs).
2.28      New management plans for the three sanctuaries would be prepared by
FD's Environmental Management Wing (para. 2.32), which would work in close
cooperation with specialists financed under the UNDP/FAO-supported Integrated
Resource Development of Sundarbans Reserve Forest Project (para. 1.31). The
Wing would also undertake surveys and prepare management plans for the
national parks, where natural values would warrant conservation efforts. The
project would finance additional staff for the Sundarbans sanctuaries together
with office accommodations, housing, cyclone shelters, drinking water
facilities, vehicles, office and equipment and incremental operating costs.
It would also provide US$500,000 to help finance investments in the other
natural parks, where such investments can be justified.
Institutional SuDport (Base Cost: USS15.6 million)
2.29      General SuDRort to Forest DeRartment (Base Cost: USS5.4 million).
FD's existing headquarters' organization needs to be expanded and streamlined
according to its key functions (para. 3.2). To achieve this, the structure
would be reorganized under five Wings with responsibilities for (a) planning
and monitoring, (b) forest management and operations, (c) extension,
(d) environmental management (paras. 2.32 and 2.33), and (e) administration
and finance. Details on this organization are in Annex 3 and in Chart II.
2.30      FD's field offices require strengthening to more effectively carry
out routine responsibilities, such as forest protection and maintenance.
Additional field-level staff are also needed for program development and



- 18 -
monitoring. Especially in FD's four coastal afforestation divisions, which
are presently integrated in the Sundarbans operations, the existing workload
and the work to be carried out under the project require the establishment of
a separate office headed by a CF to be responsible exclusively for the coastal
plantations. Also, to ensure effective project implementation, all
territorial CFs would need to be supported by a Deputy Conservator of Forests
responsible for supervision of program implementation.
2.31      The project would finance the incremental staff, office facilities,
equipment and transportation requirements, staff quarters to - ;ise project
staff, and incremental operating costs. The project would also finance
(a) six months of technical assistance to help FD improve its monitoring and
evaluation capabilities, and (b) consultancy needs for the project's two in-
depth implementation reviews (para. 3.17), which would require specialist
assistance in resource economics, forest management, sociology/anthropology,
nature conservation, and education and training (80 months).
2.32      Environmental Management (Base Cost: USS1.7 million).  FD has only
one research officer working full time on environmental issues. To strengthen
its environmental capability, FD would set up an Environmental Management Wing
and elevate it within the organization to the level of its other key
functions. The new Wing would aim at ensuring the long-term productivity of
forest lands through maintenance of biodiversity, soil quality and modulated
water flows. Within protected areas, the Wing would be directly responsible
for the planning and implementation of nature conservation activities. With
regard to FD's other operations, it would influence them through
(a) establishing environmental standards and criteria, which would be
incorporated into the RIMS's module for formulating alternative forest
management systems; (b) collecting environmental data, arranging periodic
environmental surveys, and conducting environmental assessments through
contractual arrangements; and (c) monitoring the environmental effects of FD's
forest operations.
2.33      The new Wing would include the following branches:
(a) an information and planning branch, which would (i) assist FD in
preparing strategic plans and integrating conservation aspects into
divisional FMPs; (ii) prepare management plans for natural parks
and sanctuaries; (iii) carry out natural resource surveys;
(iv) develop conservation education programs; (v) examine
legislation and policies affecting nature conservation; (vi)
provide technical support to national conservation centers such as
the National Botanical Gardens and the National Zoo; and (vii)
coordinate the introduction of environmental parameters into GIS,
which would take into account the relationship of tribal groups and
other populations residing in or adjacent to forests for which FMPs
are being prepared (para. 2.9);
(b)  a conservation oRerations branch, which would implement
conservation activities in sanctuaries and nature parks; and
(c) an environmental management branch, which would (i) develop
criteria and institute environmental standards that forestry
operations must achieve; (ii) develop procedures for achieving
environmental standards and monitor compliance; (iii) prepare a



- 19 -
field manual on best environmental practices for field operations;
and (iv) develop procedures for environmental screening and
environmental assessments of FD operations and carry them out, as
required.
2.34      The project would finance the salaries of the incremental staff
required for establishing and operating the Environmental Management Wing and
implementing the component. It would also provide the office and transport
facilities, and the furniture, equipment and incremental operating costs
needed for the proper functioning of the Wing. In addition, the project would
finance the cost of resource surveys and studies and provide residential
quarters for some of FD's environmental staff. It would also provide funds
for tours to other countries in the region to study environmental management
in production forests and management of protected areas. The new Wing would
require considerable technical assistance and training, especially during its
establishment and early operation. The project would finance the services of
specialists in strategic planning (5 months), conservation management planning
(10 months), environmental standards for forestry operations '3 months),
natural resource surveys (5 months), conservation education (4 months),
captive fauna management (2 months), and botanical garden management
(2 months). The project would further provide technical assistance for
reviewing and making recommendations to improve existing legislation and
policies on nature conservation (3 months). It would also finance the
services of a nature expert to help FD evaluate the natural values of various
protected areas, and prepare protection programs where these could be
justified (10 months).
2.35      Professional Foresters' Education (Base Cost: USS2.7 million).  To
meet FD's expanding need for professional foresters and assist in the proposed
broadening of IFCU's education programs (para. 1.18), the project would
continue the support extended to IFCU under the Second Forestry Project. In
particular, it would fiinance the expansion of facilities to include a civil
works program for two new floors to the academic block, a third floor to the
student hostel, and a residential apartment block. It would also finance site
development, access roads, a water supply system and a field station.
Technical assistance would be engaged for revising the B.Sc. curriculum
(1 month) and for preparing M.Sc. program guidelines (2 months). In addition,
the project would provide local scholarships for M.Sc. candidates and ten
overseas fellowships for Ph.D. degree education of university staff in the
areas of social forestry, social science, wood science, forest economics, land
use management, forest mensuration/inventory, environmental science, forest
engineering, and forest soils. The proiect would also finance teaching
materials, book publications, laboratory materials, vehicles, visiting
lecturers, study tours, and incremental operating costs.
2.36      In-service Training (Baoc Cost: USS1.6 million).  There is an
urgent need for more in-service training than is now provided by FD. To meet
this need, facilities and teaching staff of the Forest College in Chittagong
would be upgraded so that the College could provide a series of in-service
training programs including (a) orientation courses for newly appointed
professional officers, (b) promotion courses, and (c) short courses covering a
range of technical and administrative topics. A consultant would be engaged
for two months to assess the adequacy of the present in-service courses, and
prepare curricula for the orientation, promotion, and short courses.
Laboratories would be refurbished and additional laboratory facilities would



- 20 -
be constructed. Student dormitories and catering service areas would be
enlarged, and staff housing would be increased for a projected new staff of
13 teachers and 16 junior personnel. Fifty-seven months of fellowship
training would be designated for the teaching staff, emphasizing fields such
as rural sociology, extension, management planning, and remote sensing. To
improve practical training both in the field and laboratory, the project would
provide transport facilities and equipment.
2.37      Foresters' Training (Base Cost: USS2.7 million).  FD's decision to
change its entry level and training requirements for foresters (pat-a. 3.6)
considerably increases the need for forester training facilities and teaching
staff. FD has also decided to change the location of foresters' training from
Sylhet to Chittagong to bring the school closer to most of its field
operations. The project would finance a new forest school in Chittagong,
which would provide a three-year technical course and have capacity to admit
50 students annually. The civil works program would include the construction
of (a) a main building where classrooms, offices, dining and recreation
facilities would be located; and (b) staff quarters, site development, and
power and water supply systems. In addition, teaching and field equipment,
vehicles, books and periodicals would be provided under the project. The
project would also finance technical assistance services (2 months) to help FD
develop a new, three-year curriculum, since the existing one is based on a
two-year diploma course. The project would finance the salaries of the
additional teachers required by the school, and incremental operating costs.
2.38      Forest Guard Training (Base Cost: USSO.2 million).  Forest guard
training has been inadequate largely due to insufficient training facilities.
With FD's decision to shift foresters' training from Sylhet to Chittagong, the
Sylhet school would become available to meet FD's forest guard training needs.
The facilities would require upgrading, however. The project would finance
the renovation of the school and an additional vehicle. It would also provide
for consultancy services (1 month) to examine the curriculum for forest guard
training, which has recently been extended from three to four months.
2.39      Mangrove Research (Base Cost: USS1.3 million).  Initiated under the
Second Forestry Project, the project would continue to support BFRI's research
program on silviculture and management of mangrove plantations. Under the
proposed project, BFRI would further strengthen its coastal plantation
research station in harisal and revitalize its Sundarbans mangrove
silviculture division in Khulna, which would also serve as the institutional
base and main counterpart entity for the mangrove research carried out under
the UNDP/FAO-supported Integrated Resource Development of Sundarbans Reserve
Forest Project (para. 1.31). With assistance from UNDP/FAO project staff and
a consultant financed under the proposed project, BFRI would also examine the
effectiveness of its current mangrove research program and revise the program,
as appropriate, to meet users' needs. The project would finance the civil
works program to establish research substations, which would include offices
and residences for staff at four locations along the coastal areas; office and
field equipment; vehicles and boats; labor and materials; and inc:emental
staff and operating costs. In addition, the project would finance the
technical assistance services of (a) a mangrove research scientist (7 months)
who would assist BFRI in the examination and revision of its mangrove research
program, and (b) an entomologist (6 months) to help BFRI with the design and
execution of an effective program to control insect infestations in mangroves.



- 21 -
E. Status of Project Preparation
2.40      FD has prepared and GOB has approved the Forest Management Plans
(FMPs) for the Sylhet, Chittagong and Cox's Bazar forest divisions. GOB has
also approved FD's manual for the management of coastal plantations and the
Forest Management Manual, of which the latter regulates the procedures for
preparing, approving, implementing and revising FMPs. The project's forest
resource expansion and maintenance activities have been designed to dovetail
with the operational program being completed in June 1992 under the Second
Forestry Project. The redesign of FD's organizational structure has been
completed, but the new organization has yet to be formally adopted. Staffing
plans for implementing the project have been prepared. For the project's
pilot scheme on people's participation in forestry development and management,
the design of an operational framework has been completed. Draft terms of
reference for the consultancy services required under the project have been
prepared. Designs for FD offices and staff quarters would be according to FD
standards. They have been tested under the Second Forestry Project and have
proved satisfactory. Preliminary designs for FD's new forest school
(para. 2.37) have been completed. GOB has included the project in its FY93
Annual Development Program. Approval by GOB of the Proiect Proforma would be
a condition of Credit effectiveness.
F. Environmental Aspects
2.41      An environmental analysis concluded that it would be unlikely for
the project to have a negative environmental impact. Improvement of the
environment would be a main project objective. In particular, the project
would (a) establish within FD the capacity to develop appropriate
environmental standards for forestry operations -- taking into account any
effect on indigenous peoples -- and to undertake environmental assessments and
formulate preventative and mitigating measures where adverse impacts arise
from such operations (paras. 2.32-2.34); (b) finance a nature conservation
component designed to expand and improve the management of wildlife
sanctuaries and other protected areas (paras. 2.27 and 2.28); and (c) support
people's participation in forest development to counter forest degradation
(paras. 2.13-2.16). The project's mangrove plantation program for accreted
lands would assist in establishing shelterbelts along the coast which would
help protect against cyclones and storm surges (para. 2.18). For industrial
plantations, the project would address soil erosion issues, and train FD's
staff in soil conservation techniques (para. 2.25).
2.42      The project would not support logging in natural forests.  Since
such logging is presently banned (para. 1.23), it is not included in the FMPs
approved by GOB. If GOB changes its policy to re-introduce logging in natural
forests, an environmental assessment would be prepared under terms of
reference, satisfactory to IDA, prior to commencing such logging pursuant to
any FMP. Also, where the assessment concludes that logging would have a
negative environmental impact, steps to be taken (including mitigating
measures) would be agreed with IDA. Agreement with GOB to this effect was
reached during negotiations.



- 22 -
G. Cost Estimates
2.43         The total project cost is estimated at Tk 2.5 billion
(US$58.7 million), including a foreign exchange component of Tk 677.4 million
(US$16.2 million) or 28.0% of the total cost. Cost estimates are based on
December 1991 prices. For plantations, costs have been estimated on the basis
of existing nursery, planting and protection tenhniques. Cost estimates for
civil works are based on contractors' quotations. The costs of vehicles,
equipment and materials are based on prices quoted by suppliers. Physical
contingencies of Tk 77.2 million (US$2.0 million) have been included at the
rate of 5% of civil works, equipment, plantation labor and materials. Price
contingencies and foreign exchange rates have been estimated on the basis of
foreign and local inflation rates as follows: (a) foreign inflation at 3.9%
per annum, and (b) local inflation at 8% for FY92, 7% for FY93, and 6%
thereafter. Table 2.3 summarizes the project cost estimates by component.
Details are in Annex 4.

H. Project Financing
2.44         The project would be financed by IDA and GOB.   The IDA Credit of
SDR 36.4 million (US$49.6 million equivalent) would cover about 90% of total
project costs, net of duties and taxes. It would be channelled by GOB to MOEF
in the form of budget grants, and MOEF would pass on the funds to FD, BFRI and



- 23 -
IFCU through its regular budget process. The Credit would finance civil
works, transport and other equipment, materials and labor, technical
assistance, surveys, services, training, and incremental staff salaries and
operating costs. GOB would finance the remaining project costs, including
US$3.3 million in duties and taxes. GOB's contribution would be used mainly
to fund incremental staff and operating costs, and the labor input of groups
involved in the project's pilot scheme for participatory forest development
(paras. 2.13-2.16). The latter would be covered under WFP's Food-for-Work
Program with Bangladesh. Suitable arrangements with WFP, along the lines
established under an afforestation scheme supported by the ADB/UNDP-financed
Upazila Afforestation and Nursery Development Project (para. 1.30), would be
completed by September 30, 1992. Table 2.4 summarizes the project's financing
plan:
