2.-45     Of 1,602 incremental staff recruited under the Second Forestry
Project, 800 are estimated to be required on a continuous basis to ensure the
sustainability of that project and the effective functioning of FD.  Funding
for these staff has to be transferred from GOB's Development Budget to the
Revenue Budget to secure their continued employment. An understanding was
reached during negotiations that GOB would carry out a detailed review by
December 31, 1992 to determine the exact number of staff to be transferred to
the Revenue Budget, and discuss the findings with IDA by January 31, 1993.
2.46      Implementation of both the first and second IDA-assisted forestry
projects has been hampered by frequent delays in the release of budgetary
funds. As forestry operations are seasonal and therefore extremely sensitive
to delays, it is essential that such delays do not recur. Agreement was
reached during negotiations that FD adogt aRpropriate Drocedures for RreRarina
and aRoroving the annual program and budget and for the release cf funds.
Suitable procedures were discussed and agreed during negotiations.
I. Procurement
2.47      Procurement arrangements would be in accordance with IDA's
guidelines. Most of the civil works would be small, labor intensive,
geographically scattered (many in areas with difficult access), and spread
over six years. The largest civil works contract to be let under the project
would be for about US$1.5 million and would not be attractive for
international bidders. Civil works contracts would therefore be awarded
generally under locally advertised competitive bidding (LCB) procedures
satisfactory to IDA. In cases where worki are very small and dispersed, or



- 24 -
where no contractors can be found, construction would be done by force
account.  Such works would be limited to an aggregate of US$1 million.
2.48      Goods to be procured under the project would consist of vehicles
and boats; office, communication and laboratory equipment; furniture; tools
and field equipment; and plantation, office and educational materials.
Vehicles, speedboats, trawlers, and computer, laboratory and communication
equipment would be bulked, where possible, and procured in accordance with
international competitive bidding (ICB) procedures. Contracts for bicycles
and wooden boats would be small, with local designs most suitable to meet
local conditions. Contracts would also be small for tools, furniture and
materials, and bulking would not be practical. The size of such contracts
would not individually exceed US$150,000. Contracts for items estimated to
cost less than US$150,000 per contract may be procured under LCB procedures,
satisfactory to IDA; and goods costing less than US$20,000 per contract may be
procured after soliciting quotations from at least three independent
suppliers, up to an aggregate amount of US$1.0 million. Specialized items,
such as books, software and seeds, may be procured using direct contracting
from qualified suppliers, up to an aggregate amount of US$100,000. In bid
evaluations under ICB procedures, eligible domestic manufacturers would
receive a preference of 15X or the import duty, whichever is lower.
2.49      Service contracts for aerial photography would be let under ICB
procedures (US$1.0 million) in line with IDA's Procurement Guidelines.
Technical assistance services and arrangements for overseas training would be
on terms and conditions satisfactory to IDA, in accordance with IDA's
Guidelines for the Use of Consultants. For the project's pilot scheme on
participatory forest development, FD would enter into service contracts with
NGOs with extensive experience in this area. The services of a national or
international NGO would be contracted to provide advisory assistance and carry
out a socio-economic survey in the project area (US$0.4 million). Local-level
NGOs would be engaged for organizing and supervising pilot operations
(US$0.2 million). The other technical assistance services -- comprising a
team of natural resource experts and various other specialists recruited
internationally (173 months) and locally (155 months) -- would be combined as
much as practical, and contracts would be awarded to qualified consultancy
firms.  Annex 5 provides details on the specialties required and the areas in
which available local expertise is adequate. Total cost of these services
would be US$3.9 million. Agreement was reached during negotiations that a
technical assistance team with membershiR and terms of reference satisfactory
to IDA be established by December 31. 1992.
2.50      Labor would be required for plantation establishment, forest
management, nature conservation and environmental management. Labor would be
hired by force account or using farmers under contract in accordance with FD's
normal procedures. Similarly, engagement of incremental staff and procurement
involved in covering incremental operating expenditures would be carried out
under FD's, BFRI's, and IFCU's normal procedures, which are satisfactory.
2.51      Procurement would be the responsibility of FD, BFRI, and IFCU.
Experience under the first two IDA-assisted forestry projects has shown that
these agencies are generally capable of carrying out procurement in a
satisfactory manner. However, FD has little experience with the engagement of
NGOs. Guidelines for contractual arrangements with participating NGOs were
therefore discussed and agreed during negotiations.



- 25 -
2.52       For all items procured under ICB procedures (1OX of the IDA Credit)
and for all other contracts for civil works, goods and services estimated to
cost the equivalent of US$200,000 or more, bid documents would be submitted to
IDA for prior review, and IDA's concurrence would be sought before awarding
contracts.  This procedure would apply to some 20 contracts which would
involve about 60X of the civil works, goods and services financed by IDA. The
remaining contracts would be subject to selective ex-post review by IDA. One
in about 30 contracts would be so reviewed. For technical assistance .
contracts and overseas training, terms of reference, short lists and draft
contracts would be furnished to IDA for concurrence; and IDA's concurrence
would be sought prior to contract award. Methods of procurement are
summarized in Table 2.5 (details by year are in Annex 6):

J. Disbursement
2.53       Disbursement of the IDA Credit would cover:
(a)  85% of the expenditures for civil works, including construction
materials, architects' fees and supervision;



- 26 -
(b) 100% of the expenditures for plantation works (except labor inputs
by beneficiary groups participating in the pilot scheme for
participatory forest development) .5/;
(c) 100% of the foreign expenditures for directly imported goods; 100%
of the ex-factory price of goods manufactured in Bangladesh; and
70% of the local expenditures for goods procured locally;
(d) 100% of the expenditures for service contracts, consultant
services, fellowships and training; and
(e)  60% of the incremental recurrent costs -- including incremental
staff salaries, and operating and maintenance costs -- through June
30, 1996; and 40% thereafter.
2.54      Full documentation would be sent to IDA in support of withdrawal
applications, except for the following: items (b) and (e) above; civil works
executed under force account; and small local contracts under (a) and (c)
above, costing less than the equivalent of US$100,000. For these items,
withdrawal applications would be supported by statements of expenditures, with
documentation retained by FD, BFRI and IFCU, and made available for review by
IDA on request. This documentation would also be subject to independent
audit. All documents sent to IDA in support of withdrawal applications woulc
be certified by the CCF.
2.55      GOB would open and thereafter maintain a Special Account in Taka
with a bank, acceptable to IDA, to facilitate the payment of expenditures.
The authorized allocation for the Special Account would be US$2.2 million,
which represents an estimated four months of IDA-financed expenditures. The
Account would be administered under terms and conditions satisfactory to IDA,
and would be used only for IDA's share of project financing. It would be
replenished periodically, either monthly or whenever the balance of the
authorized allocation falls below 401, whichever occurs first. The Credit
would close on December 31, 2000. An estimated Schedule of Disbursements is
in Annex 7.
K. Accounts and Audits
2.56      Under the first two IDA-assisted forestry projects in Bangladesh,
the three implementing agencies (FD, BFRI and IFCU) have demonstrated their
ability to keep adequate records. For the proposed project, they would
establish and maintain separate project accounts to record the project's
physical progress and financial transactions. Individually identifiable
accounts would also be kept for (a) all expenditures for which Credit
withdrawals are made on the basis of statements of expenditures, and (b) the
flow of funds from the Special Account. Project accounts, including the
Special Account and the statements of expenditures would be audited annually
by independent auditors acceptable to IDA. Audited accounts, together with
auditors' comments, would be sent to IDA within nine months of the close of a
financial year. At present, the accounts of the three project agencies are
1/ Participating groups would receive coupons for their labor input, which
would be redeemed for commodities provided under WFP's Food-for-Work Program.

audited by the Comptroller and Auditor-General of Bangladesh. This
arrangement, which is satisfactory to IDA, would apply to this project.
Agreement on the above was reached during negotiations.
III. ORGANIZATION AND MANAGEMENT
A. Prolect Organization
3.1       Ministra of Environment and Forests.  MOEF would have overall
responsibility for project implementation (an implementation schedule is in
Annex 8).  To assist MOEF in this task, a Project Steering Committee would be
set up under the chairmanship of the Secretary, MOEF, with representatives
from FD, BFRI and IFCU. The Committee would (a) ensure timely availability of
funds; (b) examine implementation problems and get them resolved; (c) ensure
the adequacy of annual work programs; and (d) monitor project progress and
performance, for which it would be provided with required reports, including
semi-annual progress reports. The Committee would meet semi-annually and at
other times, as necessary, throughout the year. MOEF's Planning and
Monitoring Cell would provide the secretariat to the Committee. The Cell was
established in 1991 with seven professional posts sanctioned. Appointment of
staff to the Cell has been slow, however, with the result that little progress
has been made in overcoming MOEF's weaknesses in providing overall guidance
and coordination to the sector, and in addressing inter-sectoral issues
(para. 1.11). Full staffing of the Cell would be essential for addressing
these problems and for meeting the monitoring and coordination needs of the
proposed project. Agreement was reached during negotiations that the Cell be
fully staffed by October 31. 1992. Establishment of the Project Steering
Committee would be a condition of Credit effectiveness.
3.2        Forest Department.  Day-to-day project implementation would be the
responsibility of FD, BFRI and IFCU. FD would (a) prepare, update, revise (as
appropriate) and implement FMPs; (b) expand RIMS; (c) undertake the project's
plantation and nature conservation activities; (d) carry out the pilot scheme
on participatory forest development; (e) restructure its organization and
establish an Environmental Management Wing; and (f) strengthen its forest
education and training activities. Within FD, the CCF would have the overall
responsibility for the implementation of FD's project activities (para. 1.12).
In the field, implementation of FMPs, including the establishment and
maintenance of project plantations, adherence to environmental standards,
construction of service roads and firebreaks, and demarcation of forest
blocks/subblocks, would be the responsibility of the territorial CFs who would
discharge this responsibility through the respective DFOs and the concerned
range and beat officers (para. 1.13). Each CF would monitor and evaluate the
implementation of the FMPs for which he is responsible. CFs would receive
monthly reports from DFOs, verify information and prepare a consolidated
report for the CCF.
3.3       The organization of FD's headquarters would be restructured in
order to (a) improve planning and monitoring, (b) strengthen accountability,
(c) provide support to new and enhanced responsibilities, and (d) reduce the
Department's excessive reliance on the CCF for control and decision-making.
Under the new structure, the CCF would be assisted by four DCCFs, respectively
responsible for (i) planning, monitoring and evaluation; (ii) forest
management and operations; (iii) extension; and (iv) environmental management.



- 28 -
In addition, a CF responsible for administration and finance would report to
the CCF. The new structure is presented in Chart II. Annex 3 provides a
summary of the various responsibilities under the new organization.  Aggeement
was rached durlng negotiations that the revised organization be adoRted by
October 31. 1992. and staff to fill the key positions in the new structure be
selected by December 31. 1992.
3.4       FD's Extension Wing would be in charge of implementing the pilot
scheme for participatory forest development. It would set up a small pilot
project unit to be headed by an ACF. The unit would contract the services of
an advisory NGO (para. 2.13) and organize implementation of the scheme through
the CF and regular field staff responsible for forestry operations in the
Chittagong and Cox's Bazar forest divisions. The field staff would work in
close collaborationi with local NGOs who would be engaged to assist in field
implementation. Agreement was reached during negotiations that FD enter into
contracts with NGOs. satisfactory to IDA. by October 31. 1992.
3.5       To carry out the project's nature conservation arcivities, FD would
establish the new Environmental Management Wing (para. 2.32) and engage the
consultants financed under the project to ensure effective operation of this
Wing. FD's Forest Management Operations Wing would carry out the project's
development program for technical training, including in-service training and
training of foresters and guards. This would require that FD establish at its
Chittagong Forest College and its forest schools a core teaching staff
consisting of appropriately trained teachers with suitable minimum tenure.
Agreement was reached during-negotiations that this be achieved by
December_31. 1992.
3.6       FD's operational effectiveness will depend to a large extent on its
ability to upgrade the professional and technical skill level of its
operations. MOEF has therefore decided to change the recruitment and staffing
policy for FD's professional and technical personnel, and establish the
following two-tier forestry staff structure: (a) professional foresters to be
be recruited at the level of ACF; and (b) forest technicians with ten years of
basic education (followed by a three-year training at FD's forest schools), to
be recruited at the level of forester. Professional foresters would be
selected on the basis of criteria which would give adequate weight to
knowledge in forestry subjects. MOEF has also decided to discontinue the
recruitment of rangers for filling range officer positions. Range officers
would henceforth be recruited as professional foresters at the level of ACF.
Staffing of range offices with rangers would be gradually eliminated through
attrition. Agreement was reached during negotiations that the new recruitment
and staffing Rolicy be Rut into effect by October 31. 1992.
3.7       In the future, FD's forest operations would be regulated by and
carried out strictly in accordance with appropriate FMPs (para. 2.6).
Agreement on this was reached during negotiations. Agreement was also reached
that FMPs be preyared. implemented and revised in accordance with a Forest
Management Manual. satisfactory to IDA.  FD has recently prepared auch a
Manual (para. 2.6).
3.8       Institute of Forestry of the Chittaeong University.  The Director
of IFCU would be responsible for implementing the project's provisions for
professional foresters' education. While IFCU, as part of the University of
Chittagong, would re-i-a   nder      conttrcl of the university's governing board



- 29 -
within the Ministry of Education, project funds would be channelled through
MOEF which would approve annual budgets, ensure adequate allocation of funds
to IFCU, and monitor the implementation of this project activity. MOEF would
earmark funds for this project element under a separate budget allocation.
The Director, IFCU, would report quarterly to the Secretary, MOEF, on
implementation progress.
3.9       Bangladesh Forest Research Institute.  The Director of BFRI would
be responsible for implementing the project's mangrove research program. Day-
to-day management of the program would be the responsibility of the DFOs in
charge of the coastal plantation research station in Barisal and of the
mangrove silviculture division in Khulna. They would prepare quarterly
progress reports and submit them to the Secretary, MOEF, through the Director,
BFRI. MOEF would channel project funds to BFRI under a separate budget
heading.
3.10      BFRI would closely coordinate its project activities with the
implementation of the mangrove research program being supported under the
UNDP/FAO-supported Integrated Resource Development of the Sundarbans Reserve
Forest Project (para. 1.31). A program for cooperation between BFRI and the
UNDP/FAO Project staff would be worked out with the Chief Technical Officer
recently appointed under the UNDP/FAO Project. To ensure effective
implementation of the mangrove research provisions made under both the
proposed project and the UNDP/FAO-assisted operation, the mangrove
silviculture division in Khulna needs to be fully staffed early on during
project implementation. Agreement was reached during negotiations that such
staffing would be completed by December 31. 1992.
B. Control Over Newly Accreted Land and Land Use Coordination
3.11      The project would support mangrove afforestation on about 23,800 ha
of newly accreted land (para. 2.17). To secure the viability of investments
in such afforestation, the Ministry of Land (MOL) would need to formally
allocate to FD all land subject to plantation establishment. The allocation
of the land to FD should be for a period until plantation maturity, or through
the establishment of a reserved forest area. A Government order to this
effect needs to be issued. (A similar order, providing for the allocation of
100,000 acres of newly accreted coastal areas to FD was issued in connection
with the Second Forestry Project.) To ensure that accreted land allocated to
FD until plantation maturity is used optimally in the longer term as well, the
order would also specify that FD would return the land to MOL for reallocation
when the plantations are harvested or the land is no longer suitable for
mangroves.
3.12      To assist MOL in defining priorities for its program of surveying
newly accreted land and to address problems in allocating such land, an
allocation committee comprising representatives of MOL, MOEF and other
concerned Ministries would be established. The composition, functions and
authority of the committee would be specified in the above-mentioned
Government order (para. 3.11). A draft order was discussed and agreed upon
during negotiations. Issuance of an order. satisfactory to IDA. and
establishment of the allocation committee would be conditions of Credit
effectiveness.



- 30 -
C. Forestry Sector Master Plan
3.13      Preparation of the Forestry Sector Master Plan has begun under the
ADB/UNDP/FAO-financed Forestry Sector Master Plan Project (para. 1.32). The
Plan is expected to be completed by 1993. While IDA is closely associated
with the preparation of the Plan, and while it is not anticipated that the
Plan would make recommendations contrary to the policies and activities
supported by the proposed project, agreement was reached during negotiations
that GOB would not adogt any Plan recommendation that might have a material
and adverse impact on the imRlementation of the proposed proiect.
D. Monitoring. Evaluation and Supervision
3.14      Monitoring and Evaluation.  M&E of FD operations would be the
responsibility of the DCCF, Planning. An improved M&E system specifying the
responsibilities and performance criteria for DCCFs, CFs, and DFOs would be
designed with the assistance of an M&E consultant (para. 2.31). The system
would establish a calendar of operations and include program development
procedures, responsibilities of staff at various levels, categories of
information required, formats of reports, and the timing of information flow.
It would also be designed to trigger management action at critical points.
Detailed procedures would be prepared for recording costs and assessment of
performance, both quantitative and qualitative.
3.15        At the start of plantation operations, a plantation registry
would be opened detailing the date, material inputs and cost of each separate
activity. All nurseries would maintain a nursery register to record the
timing, the labor and the material costs. Registers would also include
records on the dispatch of planting stock and the number of seedlings
available in the nursery. By the end of each operational year, the beat
officer, supervised by the range officer, would survey all operational
results, including areas planted and treated and the survival rate and vigor
of the plantations. This information would be endorsed by the DFO and
submitted to the CF who, In turn, would endorse and convey it to the CCF.
Information submitted to the CCF pertaining to annual plantation achievement
would be checked and confirmed by the respective management plan office before
it is entered into RIMS to update the plantation register and other records.
The DCCF, Planning, would also monitor and compare to project targets the
progress in (a) preparing inventories and FMPs, (b) FD staffing, (c) technical
training, (d) implementing the pilot scheme on participatory forest
development, and (e) establishing and operating FD's Environmental Management
Wing.
3.16      Monitoring the progress in the project components implemented by
BFRI and IFCU would be the responsibility of the Planning and Monitoring Cell
in MOEF (para. 3.1). The Cell would also collate and analyze the semi-annual
project progress reports prepared by FD, BFRI and IFCU.   These reports would
be endorsed by MOEF and submitted to IDA. The reports, together with the
Monitoring and Planning Cell's analyses, would provide the basis for the
discussions at the semi-annual Project Steering Committee meetings.
3.17      To examine project progress, GOB would carry out two detailed
implementation reviews at the end of the third and fifth project years. MOEF
would organize the reviews and prepare terms of reference in consultation with
IDA. The reviews would focus on the following areas: forest land use and



- 31 -
allocation; people's participation in the management, use and protection of
forest resources; progress in the conservation of forest resources and
biodiversity, and in wildlife management; effectiveness of the training and
education programs; progress in plantation establishment and institutional
development; effectiveness of the M&E system; and progress in mangrove
research. The reviews would provide a detailed examination of problems, and
make recommendations for improving project implementation and for modifying
the project, as appropriate. The project would finance consultancy services
required to ensure the effectiveness of these reviews (para. 2.31). Agreement
was reached during negotiations that GOB undertake these reviews. discuss the
findings with IDA. and revise the Project Proforma. as necessary, to promptly
reflect project revisions agreed with IDA.
3.18      Supervision.  Supervision of project implementation by IDA would
require staff resources in line with IDA's normal supervision coefficient,
except during the first year if project implementation and in FY95 and FY97,
when the two in-depth project reviews would be carried out (para. 3.17). In
the first project year, additional resources would be required for a project
launch workshop to get implementation off to a good start. The workshop would
concentrate particularly on environmental management and people's
participation in forest development, two areas in which FD has little
experience and know-how. A supervision plan is in Annex 9.
IV. PRODUCTION. MARKETING AND PRICES
A. Yields and Production
Yield Estimates
4.1       Yields have been estimated on the basis of (a) provisional yield
tables, which have been prepared taking into consideration recent inventories
and yield data from other countries in the region; (b) field observations made
under the Second Forestry Project; and (c) literature review. Details on
estimated growth and yields for the different types of plantations are in
Annex 10, Table 1. Based on these estimates, expected thinning and final
harvest yields from different types of plantations are summarized in
Table 4.1. The estimated mean annual increment would be 13 m3/ha for all
mangrove plantations, and 25 m3/ha, 18 m3/ha, and 9 m3/ha for industrial
plantations managed under 12-, 18- and 40-year rotation, respectively. For
mangrove afforestation, it has been assumed that 40% of the plantations would
be under 10-year rotation and the balance under 17-year rotation. For
industrial plantations, 10% of the plantations would be managed on a 12-year
rotation, 40% on an 18-year rotation, and 50% on a 40-year rotation. Keora
would be the principal tree species for mangrove planting, with Baen
accounting for about only 5X of total planting stock. For long-rotation
industrial plantations, Garjan would be the principal tree species supported
by limited planting of Dhakijam, Chapalis, Mahogany, Kadam, and Teak. The
tree species for the short-rotation industrial plantations would be Acacias
and Eucalyptus.

Production
4.2       A total of 32,900 ha of new mangrove and 13,350 ha of long-rotation
and 13,530 ha of short-rotation industrial plantations would be established
under the project. Harvesting these plantations would take place over
46 years, with smallwood (firewood and pulpwooe) production starting in 1998,
pole production in 2000, and peeler production in 2005. The output of sawlogs
would peak in 2032. Annual production projected from the plantations is shown
in Annex 10, Table 2. The total production from new plantations over the life
of the project is estimated at 3.9 million m3 of sawlogs, 0.5 million m3 of
peeler logs, 4.9 million m3 of poles, and 5.8 million m3 of smallwood.
4.3       Thinning and other silvicultural treatment of existing plantations
(para. 2.11) would be expected to increase the yields of treated plantations.
However, incremental production from these activities has not been considered.
The additional production from maintenance provided for young plantations
established under the Second Forestry Project has also not been estimated.
B. Markets
4.4       General.  Given the shortage of wood and wood products in
Bangladesh (para. 1.6), there would be adequate demand for the project's
output, which would be expected to be consumed entirely within Bangladesh.
4.5       Fuelwood.  Fuelwood is a source of energy for both rural and low
income urban areas. It is also used by industries, especially by brickmakers,
despite an existing prohibition against the use of fuelwood for brick burning.
In urban areas it is sold through private wood sellers, while in rural areas
it is mainly obtained from homesteads, roadside plantations and adjacent
forests -- in many cases illegally. Project plantations would help ease the
shortage of fuelwood throughout the country. Part of the project's fuelwood
would go to local communities when benefit-sharing systems are developed and
adopted. The rest would be sold in the market. Fuelwood from mangrove
plantations would be sold locally and also transported to urban areas to be
sold through private contractors. Fuelwood from industrial plantations would
be felled by private contractors and sold in urban markets.
4.6       Poles.  The pole crop would be used mainly for house construction
and as transmission/telecommunication poles. The crop would also be used for
anchor poles and sleepers for railways. Project output would be sold as
standing timber by auction. Before the auction, FD would extract the poles
which are suitable for use by state agencies responsible for electrification,



- 33 -
communication and transport. These poles would be sold by FD to BFIDC, who
would treat and resell them to state agencies.
4.7       peeler Logs.  Peeler logs are used for the production of pulpwood,
matches and, more importantly, plywood. Bangladesh's plywood production is
about 1 million m3, of which some 85X goes into tea chest manufacturing.   The
current production of tea chests is not adequate to meet the needs of the
local tea industry, which imports tea chests from India. Most of the
project's peeler log production would therefore be expected to be sold to the
local tea industry, and the rest to match factories.
4.8       Sawlogs.  Sawlogs produced from project plantations would be sold
to sawmillers. The estimated combined annual capacity of the existing
1,600 sawmills is 5 million m3, while present total sawlog production is only
about 2 million m3 due to insufficient raw material. The project's entire
output of sawlogs would be purchased by private sawmills, as BFIDC's mills are
serviced from the forest areas in the Chittagong Hill Tracts.
C. Prices
4.9       Prices Charged to Private Sector.  FD sells most of its wood
production to the private sector through open auction. The wood is either
extracted by FD and then auctioned from its depot, or FD auctions tlhe standing
timber, in which case the buyer pays FD the stumpage value and extracts the
wood on his own account. Average auction prices at the end of 1991 and
stumpage prices are in Table 4.2:
Table 4.2: Wood Prices (Tk/ft3)
Auction Price
at FD DeRot       Stumpage Price
Fuelwood
Mangrove                   18                   7
Industrial                 24                  11
Poles
House poles               120                  70
Transmission poles        160                 110
Peeler logs                 190                 120
Sawlogs
Short-rotation            235                 155
Long-rotation             350                 260
Present market prices are well above comparable international prices due to
the overall shortage of wood in Bangladesh and the existing Government ban on
felling in natural forests (para. 1.23).
4.10      Prices Charged to Public Sector.  FD is selling wood and
concessions to state enterprises at prices which are in many cases
considerably below market. For instance, BCIC's Khulna Newsprint Mill pays
only Tk 2.2 per m3 stumpage for Gewa wood produced in Sundarbans and used for
newsprint -- less than 1X of the market price. As other examples, BFIDC has
paid Tk 3,500 per m3 for Sundri poles at its mill gate, while the same poles
would have fetched twice that amount in the market; and BCIC's Sylhet Pulp and
Paper Mill has paid Tk 230 per m3 for Melokana wood, about 30X of the
comparable FD auction price. GOB has now decided to eliminate these price



- 34 -
distortions. Agreement was reached during negotiations that prices charged to
state-owned enterpriges for forest products and concession arrangements be set
from now on at market level. except for sales tc BCIC-owned mills. For these
mills, prices would be set at 40X of market by July 1. 1992: 80% of market by
July 1. 1993: and 100Z of market by July 1. 1994. ImDlementation of this
Rricing schedule would be a condition of Credit effectiveness.  In addition,
an understanding was reached during negotiations that the prices would (a) be
set once a year and remain fixed for one year, and (b) be determined on the
basis of the average auction price obtained by FD during the previous year.
Where auction prices are not available, prices would be set on the basis of
market prices for similar products in the market, taking into consideration
transport, harvesting and other costs that need to be considered to make the
products sold by FD comparable to those available in the market.
V. BENEFITS. JUSTIFICATION AND RISKS
A. Project Benefits
5.1       The main direct benefit from the project would be the production of
wood, which would help maintain the country's supply for industrial use and
fuel consumption. Total incremental production over the project's life is
estimated at about 15 million m3 (para. 4.2) with an economic value of about
US$652 million. The foreign exchange impact of the project's output is
difficult to quantify since Bangladesh's external trade in wood and wood
products is minimal. However, the increased availability o;Z fuelwood would
reduce the use of imported kerosene. Similarly, with an increased fuelwood
supply, agricultural waste Droducts; which are being used for fuel, would be
available for fodder, and dung could be used as fertilizer. The project would
also provide 1,150 permanent staff positions and create labor requiz'ements for
about 83,500 person years. It would further result in substantial
institutional improvements to help ensure sustainable and environmentally
sound forest development. Specifically, it would:
(a) increase FD's capability as a renewable resource management agency
by (i) improving and expanding training and education of staff at
both the technical and professional level, (ii) providing modern
forest management tools in the form of a GIS-based information
management system, and (iii) establishing an improved organization;
(b) improve FD's ability to manage forests in an ecologically sound way
by establishing an environmental management capability within the
Department, and by financing the investments, plans and studies
required to expand nature conservation activities;
(c) develop models for effective participation by local communities in
forest management planning, use and protection; and
(d) strengthen Bangladesh's forestry training and research capability.



- 35 -
B. Economic Analysis
Methodology
5.2       The economic analysis for the project has been carried out for the
project as a whole and separately for (a) mangrove afforestation,
(b) industrial plantation establishment, and (c) the silvicultural treatment
of existing plantations. (No economic analysis has been prepared for the
project components on nature conservation and institutional support.) The
analysis is based on the following main parameters:
(a) General. Prices have been expressed in December 1991 constant
Taka. International prices have been converted at an exchange rate
of US$1.00 - Tk 38.8.
(b) Valuation of Costs. For traded cost components, project costs have
been valued on the basis of border prices. Non-traded components,
including labor, have been adjusted by the standard conversion
factor of 0.8. Physical contingencies have been included.
(c) Valuation of Benefits. The project's incremental wood production
has been valued on the basis of economic prices. In the case of
sawlogs, peeler logs and poles, these have been calculated on the
basis of the Bank's price forecast for Malaysian Meranti round logs
for the year 2000, and a 33% price deduction to account for the
lower quality of Bangladesh logs. In the case of fuelwood, the
economic price has been calculated based on the assumption that 80%
of the project's fuelwood output would replace imported kerosene,
and 20% would replace other domestic energy sources. Details of
the calculations are in Annex 11, Tables 1 and 2.
(d) Proiect Period. For new plantations, an eleven-year investment
period has been included to reflect the four-year maintenance
required for plantations established in the seventh project year.
Benefits are taken for forty-six years to include the harvesting of
the long-rotation plantations established in year seven of the
project.
5.3       Economic benefits considered in the analyses consist of the value
of sewlogs, peeler logs, poles and smallwood, which would be produced from the
plantations to be established under the project (Annex 11, Table 3). In
addition, for the silvicultural treatments to be carried out at existing
plantations, it has been assumed that the project would advance by two years
rhese treatments and the wood production resulting from thinning operations.
Benefits would also be reaped from the project's investment in nature
conservation and institution building, including environmental management,
training and research. Though significant, they have not been quantified.
5.4       Economic costs (Annex 11, Table 4) included in the economic
analysis for the project as a whole comprise the investment costs and
recurrent expenditures of all project components including nature conservation
and institutional support. For the plantation components, only the cost of
the respective component has been considered.



- 36 -
Economic Rates of Return
5.5       Economic Rates of Return (ERRs) for the project as a whole and for
individual components (except nature conservation and institutional support)
are presented in Table 5.1.
Table 5.1: Economic Rates of Return
Component                         IRR
Total project                     16Z
Mangrove afforestation            24%

plantations                   35X
The project's net present value discounted at the opportunity cost of capital,
estimated at 12%, would be Tk 815 million (US$21 million). Cost and benefit
streams are shown in Annex 11, Table 5.
Sensitivity
5.6       To test the sensitivity of the ERRs, plantation yields and prices
were reduced, and costs increased to a level which would yield an ERR
equivalent to the opportunity cost of capital, estimated at 12X (Annex 12).
The analysis shows that the ERR is robust. Yields or economic stumpage values
would have to fall by 401 before the project's ERR would decline to 121.
Costs would have to more than double in order to reduce the ERR to 121. If
costs were to increase by 50X and benefits to decline by 20X, the ERR would
still be 141. The ERR would also be 141 if all benefits were to be realized
one year later than expected.
C. Risks
5.7       The project may face three types of risks caused by natural events,
institutional shortcomings, and socio-economic conditions.
5.8       Natural Events.  For mangrove plantations, sedimentation in certain
parts of the Bay of Bengal can be excessive and result in burial of seedlings,
or erosion may wash away young and sometimes even established plantationr. FD
has developed considerable experience in site selection over the last 25 years
so that this risk can be kept at an acceptable level. Also, the cost of
establishing mangrove plantations includes a large contingency for replanting.
Anv loss of benefits due to erosion of established plantations can be reduced
by early harvesting. Cyclones and storm surges could also damage young and
established plantations. However, due to the large span of coastal
plantations, this damage is likely to be localized. As important, the
plantations would provide protection to inland properties which, without such
protection, might experience great losses during cyclones and storm surges.
5.9       For industrial plantations, any risk due to natural events would be
small. The plantations receive good and reliable rainfall and there is only a
limited risk of forest fire. Insect epidemics are of concern to mangrove, as
well as to Albizia species, used in industrial plantations. However,
experience indicates that insect attacks only reduce growth, and only to an



- 37 -
extent which would not threaten the economic viability of investments. As
indicated in the sensitivity analysis (para. 5.6), tree growth would have to
decline by an unlikely proportion for investments to become uneconomical.
5.10      Institutional Shortcomings.  Delays in releasing budgetary funds
and inadequate planning have hampered the efficiency and effectiveness of
field operations, especially maintenance of young plantations. To deal with
this risk, the project would streamline program preparation, budget approval
and funding processes (para. 2.46). The project would also address
shortcomings in FD's organization, and improve FD's planning, managing and
monitoring capabilities. Remaining institutional risks would be minimal.
5.11      Socio-economic Conditions.  Forests, in general, and plantations,
in particular, have been subject to pilferage, grazing damage and resistance
to forestry investments by people living in the vicinity of state forests.
The project would help FD to improve its approach to addressing socio-economic
issues so that appropriate systems with adequate incentives for local
populations could be established in each area. Also, through its pilot
operation for participatory forest development, FD would be able to formulate
a variety of incentives to realize local participation for protecting forests.
The project would thus help FD to deal effectively with socio-economic risks.
VI. AGREEMENTS REACHED AND RECOMMENDATION
6.1       During negotiations, agreements were reached with GOB that:
(a) before undertaking logging in natural forests, (i) an environmental
assessment be prepared under terms of reference satisfactory to
IDA, and (ii) where required, mitigating measures be agreed with
IDA (para. 2.42);
(b) FD adopt appropriate procedures for program and budget preparation,
approval, and funds release (para. 2.46);

satisfactory to IDA be established by December 31, 1992

BANGLADESH
FOREST RESOURCES MANAGEMENT PROJECT
The Project's Link to the Bank's Forest Sector Policy
1.        The project would be an instrument to attain the forest development
and conservation goals outlined in the World Bank's forest policy paper, dated
June 1991. In particular, it would (a) encourage sustainable resource use and
discourage deforestation; (b) expand forest resources; (c) develop models for
the participation of local people in forest management; and (d) foster nature
preservation. Under the project, all accessible forest resources would be
brought under scientific and sustainable management to maximize their
contribution to the economic, social and environmental goals of the country.
All forest management activities would be carried out according to forest
management plans, which would be continuously updated as new information
becomes available through resource inventories, socio-economic surveys and
environmental studies financed under the project. Sustainability would be the
main guiding criterion in allocating forest resources to various uses.
Institutional, technical and procedural systems, which are partly lacking or
ineffective today, would be established to apply this criterion to all
forestry decisions. Resource expansion programs supported by the project
would aim to rehabilitate degraded forest areas and lands unsuitable for other
uses. Management objectives of project plantations would be designed to
balance national and local needs. In this context, the project would support
a pilot scheme for people's participation in forest development and
maintenance. The project would also provide investments for improving the
management of proven wildlife sanctuaries, and support the identification of a
national protected area system and prepare management plans for its
development and maintenance. Institutional arrangements to be introduced by
the project would help secure preservation of nature and the environment
within the protected area system, and enhance forestry planning and operations
by inserting social and environmental aspects into the decision-making
process.
2.        For the type of activities supported under the proposed project,
the Bank's forestry policy requires that several prerequisites be met by the
concerned government in order to justify Bank/IDA involvement. These
conditions and the corresponding policies and activities supported by the
proposed project are summarized below (the Bank's conditions are underlined):
(a) adoption of Rolicies and an institutional framework to ensure
conservation and sustainable use of existing forests and to Rromote
active participation of peoRle and the private sector in the long-
term management of natural forests. The project has been designed
in accordance with a policy framework promoting planned utilization
and regeneration of forest resources; optimizing resource use by
balancing environmental, social and economic goals; developing
systems and methods to secure people's participation in planning,
managing and utilization of the forest resources; and strengthening
environmental management and nature conservation capabilities. A
major effort would be made under the project to address the current
institutional shortcomi.lgs of the Forest Department (FD), GOB's



- 40 -                       AMEX I
Page 2 of 2
main agency for managing forest resources. In addition, the
project would ensure appropriate pricing of wood and wood products;
(b) adoRtion of a comnrehensiv_ and environmentally sound forestry
conservation and development Rlan that contains a clear definition
of the roles and rigbts of the government. the private sector. and
local dwellers. A key objective of the project would be to ensure
that all of FD's forest operations are regulated by up-to-date and
scientifically prepared forest management plans. These plans would
be prepared in line with sound economic, environmental and social
goals. The project would also support the preparation and
implementation of protected area management plans. Moreover, GOB
has now commenced the preparation of a Forestry Sector Master Plan
to establish a framework for the long-term protection and
development of the forest resources. (The Plan is being prepared
under an ADB/UNDP/FAO-supported project which is being implemented
with IDA's participation as a " Party with Special Interest"). The
project's support for inventory and management planning would
assist in continuing the planning process to ensure that the Master
Plan can be updated and revised as and when required;
(c) undertaking social. economic. and environmental assessments of the
forests considered for commercial utilization. Project provisions
for inventories, surveys, data processing systems and arrangements
for decision-making would aim at optimizing the aggregated outcome
of forestry operations. The project would establish socio-economic
data as an integral part of the forest management data base and use
it in deciding on appropriate management systems which would
provide incentives to local people, where required.  The project
would support the development within FD of procedures, decision-
criteria, and technical capability for environmental assessment;
(d)  setting aside adeguate compensatory preservation forests to
maintain biodiversitv and safeguard the interest of forest
dwellers, specifically their rights to access to designated forest
areas. The project's environmental management and nature
conservation components would provide direct support for protected
areas in Sundarbans, where natural values are intact and effective
protection is feasible. It would also provide for nature surveys
and management planning to determine the technical, social and
financial feasibility of protected areas at other forest locations.
Because of security problems, the project would not include
interventions in the Chittagong Hill Tract areas; and
(e) establishing the institutional caRacity to imRlement and enforce
the above commitments. With its environment and institutional
development components, and its provisions for technological
improvements and training/education, the project would make a major
contribution to improving the institutional capacity of FD to help
ensure sustainable and conservation-oriented forestry.
3.        A key consideration in the Bank's forest sector policy is that
Bank/IDA support should not be provided for logging operations in primary
natural moist forests. The project would not support such operations.

BANGLADESH
FOREST RESOURCES MANAGEMENT PROJECT
Pilot Scheme on Participatory Forest Development
Proiect Context
1.        Over the past five years, in Bangladesh, as in other countries in
the region, there has been an increasing awareness of the need to involve
local populations in forestry development and management activities,
particularly in areas of dense and growing population. There is some limited
experience, initiated with support from national NGOs, with involving local
people in rehabilitation of degraded forest lands, but none in the area of the
proposed project.
2.        The Ministry of Environment and Forests (MOEF) wishes to include in
the project a pilot component for participatory forest management and
development, to be implemented initially in two forest divisions with hilly
forest tracts) Chittagong and Cox's Bazaar, to evaluate that pilot component
during the first mid-term review, and allocate funds to expand participatory
management activities to other areas, based on the experience gained. The
pilot component would be implemented by a small unit within the Forest
Department (FD), which would contract the services of an advisory NGO
(national or international) to assist in formulating pilot strategies,
supervise field implementation of pilot activities by field-based NGOs in the
two divisions, and monitor and evaluate pilot activities. The unit would
liaise with FD staff involved in the ADB-financed Upazila Afforestation and
Nursery Development Project, which is experimenting with participatory
forestry activities outside the state forest lands, in developing technical
models for plantation/contract reforestation, forest management, and
agroforestry.
Socio-Economic Issues Important for Particioatory Forest Management
3.        There is a diverse cultural and socio-economic situation in the two
forest divisions where activities would be carried out.   Due to the disaster-
prone nature of this part of Bangladesh, forest management strategies must be
quite responsive to specific needs of the local population for forest products
and land for alternative housing and livelihoods in the wake of the periodic
natural disasters. When major cyclones displace coastal populations and
damage housing, family assets, and infrastructure, pressure is put on the
forest estate to supply construction timber, to provide temporary or permanent
habitation, and to provide alternative sources of employment. Without a
strong stake in sustainable forestry from the surrounding population, it is
unlikely that forest resources can be developed and managed weathering such
periodic disasters.
4.        The population settled in and around the forest estate in the
inland parts of Chittagong and Cox's Bazaar is diverse. It includes villagers
of Bengali ethnic origin, indigenous peoples with distinct tribal ethnic



- 42 -
ANNEX 2
Page 2of 6
identities, and recent settlers who have been migrating into tne area in
search of employment, or fleeing natural disasters.  Some of the cultivators
settled in the area have formal land title to their agriculture and homestead
lands. Others have settled on state forest lands, cultivating rice in the
fertile forest land valleys or establishing homestead plots with houses and
fruit and vegetable homegardens. There are large proportions of landless
people, either long-term residents or recent settlers, who work as
agricultural laborers in the fields of landowners, migrate to nearby towns or
other rural areas for seasonal employment, and supplement wage income with
homegarden produce or illegal ginger, turmeric, or betel leaf cultivation on
forest lands. Some of the landless people have been encouraged to cultivate
forest land by influential landowners, who use their influence to keep
Government officials from preventing such encroachment. Landowners use this
either as a strategy to acquire new holdings or to extract rents through
informal arrangements from the landless cultivators. Timber merchants and
wealthy individuals also pay poor villagers to extract logs illegally for
commercial sale.
5.        The situation of the villagers of tribal ethnic identity is
substantially different from that of the Bengali ethnic groups. These
villagers are Buddhist or Christian and are the indigenous groups of the hilly
areas. Traditionally, these tribal ethnic groups resided in the forest areas
and practiced shifting cultivation, and extracted a variety of non-timber
forest products for their livelihood. Over the last century, as FD was given
increasing control over this land, the tribal farmers began to rely less on
shifting cultivation and became wage laborers, still collecting a variety of
non-timber forest products. The tribal groups have less political clout
within the overall society than those long-term and recent residents of
Bengali origin. They will need more support from Government and NGOs in
establishing viable forest management groups for blocks of forest under
different management systems.
6.        The current agricultural and agro-forestry practices in the forest
lands, apart from the paddy lands along the more fertile river valleys, are
generally unsustainable. Betel leaf cultivation, a popular cash crop in the
sloping lands in the southern forest division, is particularly demanding of
soil nutrients; and farmers mine surrounding lands for topsoil to add along
with purchased fertilizer to betel leaf growing sites. A forest slope cleared
for betel cultivation can be farmed, even with topsoil mining, for only two or
three years, and then is abandoned. Fertility is so depleted in these lands,
according to the FD, that when plantations are established, foresters wait a
year or two to plant seedlings in the former betel cultivation patches found
within the overall plantation area. Homestead areas usually include a variety
of fruit trees -- papaya, citrus, and banana -- and some vegetables. Some of
the forest areas are currently managed for sun grass, a thatch material with
good commercial value. FD auctions blocks of suitable forest land to private
entrepreneurs for sun grass extraction for 3-5 year periods, and these
entrepreneurs may invest inputs and labor to increase sun grass production in
these areas. The areas suitable for sun grass are limited in extent, and FD
policy is to keep such areas under this current form of management and perhaps
experiment with more long-term lease arrangements involving local
communities/groups.



* 43 -
ANNEX 2
Page 3 of 6
7.        There are a number of experiments on-going in the country in public
and private wastelands and forest lands involving local people in forestry.
These include: (a) an experiment of Bangladesh Forest Research Institute
(BFRI) in Betagi and Pomora blocks of Chittagong division for allocating
degraded forest land to groups of landless people for establishment of
perennial agroforestry, including growing of timber 1; (b) allocation of
degraded forests in Tangail district near Dhaka to landless groups under a
NGO-managed program (Proshika); (c) a World Food Program-assisted scheme to
involve local groups in agroforestry on private and public wastelands
implemented by field-based NGOs, and; (d) afforestation activities in public
wastelands under the ADB-assisted Upazila Afforestation and Nursery
Development Project, which provide landless groups with 9-30 year lease
arrangements to develop degraded public lands for agroforestry.
8.        A variety of agroforestry models have been included in these
schemes, which range from those allowing annual agriculture cropping between
rows of traditional timber species in the initial years of plantation
establishment, to those involving intensive agroforestry with various
perennial cash crops, fruit, timber, and seasonal vegetable cultivation.
Experimentation has so far taken place on fairly limited amounts of forest
land, and the more successful experiments have been concentrated on areas with
comparatively better soils. The Betagi and Pomora technical models, for
example, are not applicable to most of the hilly lands included in the
proposed project area. In Northern Bangladesh, there has been some
experimentation with cane/timber and bamboo/timber plantation models, which
allow farmers to harvest cane or bamboo on short rotation and earn additional
income from processing industries using these raw materials. There is no
practical experience in areas of lower fertility to evaluate the economic or
social viability of introducing similar technical models in the proposed
project area. The pilot project would adapt some of the principles of the
more successful models tailored to the particular ecological conditions in the
hilly forest zone.
9.        Two NGOs in Chittagong division, Pouse and Pothrikrit, have begun
afforestation schemes with WFP food-for-work resources on private wastelands
under the Upazila Afforestation and Nursery Development Project model. They
are in the initial stages of project implementation, but seem to have
successfully identified lands for afforestation, organized and trained groups
of landless laborers (mainly women) to plant and protect afforested areas, and
provided technical support in choice of technical models, advice on related
income-generating activities (mulberry silk, livestock rearing, etc.) to
maximize income from the program.
Group Organization Model - Proshika
10.       One national NGO with considerable experience in afforestation
activities is a Dhaka-headquartered NGO named Proshika. Proshika (like some
.1/ A paper has been written on this experience, Ashraf Uddin Ahmed and Abul
Asad, "Social Forestry in Bangladesh: A Case Study of Betagi and Pomora
Experiments", University of Chittagong, January 1987.



- 44 -
ANNEX 2
Page 4 of 6
other NGOs in Bangladesh who empower individuals for development initiatives
through group formation) has a strategy for group development with some
important principles relevant to the proposed pilot program. In principle,
Proshika recognizes that, in order for groups of landless or near landless to
be able to protect and manage forest resources sustainably, they must develop
their own power base and organizational capacity to keep control over the
resource vis-a-vis other members of the population and to engage in
agreements/contracts with the Government. The group formation and development
mechanisms built into Proshika's program are designed to further this aim. In
their on-going program, Proshika organizes or identifies groups a full year
prior to providing any development resources to them. The individual group
members meet weekly, and set up a small savings fund which is used to support
needs of the members and to undertake small development schemes. Oa,'e the
group proves itself to be motivated and sustainable, it is given access to
resources for various schemes.
DescriRtion of Component
11.       The pilot component would be undertaken in two or three forest
blocks in Chittagong and Cox's Bazar divisions. Areas would be identified
which represent the range of pressure on forest lands (different types of
settlers, different ethnic groups, different ecological conditions, different
range of forest-based economic activities being carried out) and groups of
settlers and local villagers involved in forest development and management.
Optional models would include: agroforestry, intensified taungya, contract
reafforestation, and joint management of forest plots with local groups and
FD. A variety of legal and sharing arrangements would be tested in the pilot
activities, some of which would be based on the experience in other NGO
schemes and the Upazila Afforestation and Nursery Development Project. Upon
completion of the pilot phase, FD would develop models for legal agreements,
including sharing arrangements to be entered into with participatory groups.
Institutional Arrangements
12.       The component would be managed by the Extension Wing of FD, and
implemented under a contractual arrangement with an advisory national or
international NGO with expertise in participatory forestry, which would
provide technical support to local-level NGOs with on-going activities in the
pilot districts for implementing the pilot work. The advisory NGO would also
undertake the socio-economic survey, and monitoring and evaluation work
required for the component.
13.       A small unit would be created within FD's Extension Wing for the
implementation of the pilot component. This pilot unit would also form the
core for expansion of participatory forestry activities after the
implementation of the pilot scheme. It would be staffed by an Assistant
Conservator of Forests (ACF) and junior officers, and provided with computer
equipment and transport facilities.



45 -
LNNEX
Page 5 of 6
Role of Advisory NGO
14.       The advisory NGO would be contracted by FD to:  (a) assist in
developing pilot models for testing various options, including technical
options for plantation and agroforestry; (b) identify appropriate local-level
NGOs to help implement the pilot schemes locally; (c) provide technical
support, including training and supervision to the local-level NGOs; (d)
monitor the progress of pilot activities; (e) carry out a socio-economic
survey of the conditions in the project area; and (f) organize and help FD in
preparations for periodic workshops on participatory forestry. Under
contract, FD would provide funds to the NGO for staff working on the project,
for costs of transportation and per diem for staff travelling to and from the
pilot areas, to cover training and workshop preparation costs, monitoring and
evaluation costs, and the costs to carry out the survey.
15.       The socio-economic survey would identify the range of socioeconomic
and ecological conditions in the forest areas covered by the forestry
management/data base survey. Multi-disciplinary teams of researchers would
undertake rapid appraisal work in the project area, supplemented by interviews
conducted by field enumerators to collect supplementary statistical
information on (a) the population resident in the forest estate area under
settlement, and (b) economic activities. Data generated by the study would be
analyzed and included in the data base maintained by the management plan
office of FD.
16.       To carry out its responsibilities under the project, the NGO would
need to engage suitable staff for planning, supervision and monitoring. For
the socio-economic survey it is tentatively estimated that the following staff
would be required: Principal Investigator (16 months); Social Scientists (2)
(24 months); Agronomists (2) (18 months); Forestry Specialists (2)
(18 months); Field Enumerators (4) (48 months); Data Processors (2)
(14 months); and Clerical Support Staff (12 months). For support to the
local-level NGOs, it is tentatively estimated that the following staff would
need to be engaged: Project Directors (2) (120 months); Assistants (2)
(120 months); Field Supervisors (10) (600 months); Group Organizers (5)
(500 months); Agronomists/Foresters (2) (60 months); Training Consultant
(30 months).
Role of Local-Level NGOs
17.       Local-level NGOs would be contracted by FD's pilot unit to organize
groups for pilot activities; assist these groups to engage in participatory
schemes, supervise the work of these groups, with the advisory NGO undertaking
training and technical backstopping; and participate in workshops to discuss
findings and adjust on-going pilot programs.
18.       Pilot activities would be undertaken in two or three blocks
representing a range of situations: different physical site capacity for
growing trees and agroforestry models, including new and less-recent settlers
and indigenous tribal groups resident in the forest estate, and including poor
villagers and communities resident outside the forest estate, but dependent on
forest resources for their local economy. In all probability, pilot



- 46 -
ANINEX 2
Page 6 of 6
activities would concentrate on degraded areas for new plantation, but pilot
or subsequent activities might include areas uider existing plantations for
local management and protection. Activities to be supervised by the NGOs
would include group formation and institution building, nursery development,
plantation maintenance, and assistance in marketing or harvesting of
intermediate or short-term produce. In some cases, it may prove useful to
experiment with income-generating or value-added processing activities related
to forest products being produced (sericulture, bamboo handicrafts, cane
products, agriculture, etc.). Funds hlAve been budgeted for such activities,
subject to their prior approval by the pilot unit in FD.
19.       Approximately 40 groups of 10-20 individuals would be involved in
project activities. Each individual would be assigned 4-5 acres per annum for
a two-year period for forest development, maintenance and protection. The
area covered under the pilot project would therefore be approximately
4,000-8,000 acres.
Proiect Financing
20.       The project would finance the establishment and operation of the
proposed pilot project unit in FD's Extension Wing. Funds would be provided
to finance additional staff and operating costs, and computer and other
equipment, and transport facilities required for implementation of this pilot
operation. Also covered would be the costs of the services to be provided by
the advisory NGO, and the costs of the implementation assistance provided by
local NGOs. To help FD review the NGO proposals and monitor the effectiveness
of their implementation, the project would provide FD with six months of
technical assistance each for the areas of agroforestry and agronomy, where FD
presently lacks expertise. Labor inputs by participating groups would be
reimbursed through the World Food Program's (WFP) Food-for-Work program, along
lines similar to the ones set up under an afforestation scheme supported by
the Upazila Afforestation and Nursery Development Project. Funds would be
channelled to NGOs in accordance with acceptable Government norms and
procedures.

BANGLA DESH
FOREST RESOURCES MANAGEMENT PROJECT
Responsibillit Distribution Under the Forest DeRartment's new Organization
1.        The Forest Department's (FD) headquarters organization would be
restructured under the project, along functional lines, in order to
(a) improve the Department's effectiveness in planning, monitoring and
evaluation; (b) strengthen accountability; (c) provide adequate support to new
and enhanced responsibilities; and (d) reduce the Department's excessive
reliance on the Chief Conservator of Forests (CCF) for control and decision
making. Under the new structure, the CCF would be assisted by four Deputy
Chief Conservators of Forests (DCCFs) responsible for (a) planning, monitoring
and evaluation; (b) forest management and operations; (c) extension; and
(d) environmental management, respectively; and by a Chief Conservator of
Forests (CF) responsible for administration and finance. The new structure is
presented in Chart II. Below is a summary of the distribution of
responsibilities under the new organization:
2.        The DCCF. Planning would be responsible for:
(a) preparing the aggregated annual work program of FD and the budget
estimates for all activities under the development budget; and
(b) monitoring and evaluating program implementation.
The DCCF, Planning would also prepare monthly progress reports for
the CCF to be discussed at monthly progress review meetings. In
addition, the DCCF, Planning would be responsible for preparing
consolidated six-monthly progress reports on the project.
3.        The DCCF. Forest Management and Operations would be responsible
for:
(a)  preparing and revising all forest management plans (FMPs), and the
technical review and supervision of FMP implementation;
(b) the technical review and supervision of the annual plantation
establishment and maintenance programs; and
(c) the supervision of education and training programs, and the
construction and maintenance of training facilities.
FD's RIMS cell, the two existing management plan offices, and the
proposed third one to be established in Khulna would work under the
direction and supervision of the DCCF, Forest Management and
Operations.
4.        The DCCF. Extension would be responsible for:
(a) developing and implementing social/community forestry programs;



- 48 -                         ANNEX 3
Page 2 of 2
(b) publications and public relations; and
(c) NGO relations and coordination.
5.        The DCCF. Environmental Management would be responsible for:
(a) developing and implementing management plans for wildlife
sanctuaries, nature parks and other protected areas;
(b) establishing environmental standards for the sector, which would be
integrated into FMPs and FD's Resource Information Management
System;
(c) developing conservation education programs; and
(d) developing procedures for, and carrying out environmental impact
assessments for sector programs and projects, and monitoring
compliance.
6.        The CF. Administration would be responsible for:
(a) personnel management;
(b) establishment management (e.g. assets);
(c) administrative and procedures development; and
(d) finance and accounting.
